# Programming Foundations: Object-Oriented Design

> All good software starts with a great design. Object-oriented design helps developers plan applications before they write a single line of code, and break down ideas into reusable and maintainable components. This course focuses on the foundational concepts, teaching them in a fun, interactive way to help you quickly develop your skills. Tag team Olivia and Barron Stone introduce you to the concep

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3) | 3h 41m
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

## Instructor

- [[Olivia Chiu Stone]]
- [[Barron Stone]]

### Introduction

#### Learn object-oriented design principles

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=0)** (keyboard clicking)
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=3)** - As someone who loves writing code, as soon as I get a new idea for an application, I jump right to programming.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=11)** - And as someone who manages software development teams, I know that's the last thing you should be doing.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=17)** Good software starts with the design, so you can avoid wasting time going down the wrong path.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=22)** I'm Olivia Stone.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=24)** - And I'm Barron Stone.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=25)** In this course, we'll introduce you to the fundamentals of object oriented analysis and design.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=30)** - It's what you should be doing to plan your program before writing a single line of code.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=35)** Object oriented programming languages have become the standard for everything from web development to running desktop applications.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=43)** With proper design, they enable you to create applications that are flexible, maintainable, and extensible.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=49)** - [Barron] We'll start with the foundational concepts and terminology you need to discuss object oriented development.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=55)** From there, we'll walk through a process to take your ideas for an application, something you'd write on the back of a napkin, and understand how to break it apart into the right pieces so you know exactly what code to write.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=67)** - Now, we won't be writing code in this course because during the design process, we don't need to.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=73)** Instead, we'll use basic components of the Unified Modeling Language, or UML, to diagram visual models of our object oriented systems.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=82)** It's how you can articulate your ideas to collaborate with others.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/learn-object-oriented-design-principles?u=76281980&t=85)** - So let's get to it.

#### What you should know
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - Although we won't be writing code in this course, we will explore a few short examples of code in several different languages.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=7)** So you should have at least a basic understanding of programming.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=11)** The fundamentals like variables and functions, loops and conditions.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=15)** - If you're already working with an object-oriented language, you've probably encountered some of the core concepts like classes and objects, but you may not be using them to their full extent, or are struggling to maintain a growing application.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=29)** - Or you might be a seasoned programmer with years of experience in non object-oriented languages.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=35)** I first learned programming with straight C and I understand that it can be difficult to make the jump to an object-oriented mindset.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=42)** If any of that describes you, this is the perfect course to begin thinking in object-oriented ways.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=48)** To unlock what your language can really do.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=51)** - This course is about the object-oriented design process.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=55)** Not programming, so our tools, we've pencil and paper, index cards, and whiteboards, not a code editor or IDE.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=62)** Now there's no one right way to go about this.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=66)** Object-oriented design is not a formal process.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=69)** It's set of ideas and techniques to incorporate into your own process.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=73)** It'll allow you to build better and more complex applications.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=77)** - We've included several challenge problems throughout this course, and even if you don't have time to sit down and create your own formal solution to them, we strongly encourage you to at least think about each of the challenges and watch our solutions to really get the most out of this course.

### 1. Object-Oriented Fundamentals

#### Object-oriented thinking
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=0)** - Many of today's most popular programming languages are object oriented, but that's not the only way to program.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=7)** To understand when and why using object oriented languages can be beneficial, it helps to compare it with a common alternative.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=15)** Procedural programming languages like plain C.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=18)** In procedural code, the program is written as a long series of operations to execute.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=23)** Now, some of that might be organized into named functions or sub-routines to make the code modular and maintainable, but the end goal is really just to get from Point A to Point B to complete some task.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=37)** It's a straight forward approach that I like to relate to writing a recipe for a cookbook.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=42)** The program or recipe to say, bake a cake, would list the sequence of steps you need to follow.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=49)** Mix the ingredients together, pour them into a cake pan, and put it in the oven.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=54)** Just execute those steps in that order and voila, a cake.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=60)** I've found that new programmers have a tendency to write code in this procedural manner because it's easy to think of simple programs in terms of sequential steps.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=69)** - Right, and to approach that same task of baking a cake in an object oriented manner rather than describing a sequence of steps, I'll describe each of the objects in my kitchen, the pan, the oven, and the mixer, and what each one can do.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=84)** So, instead of writing a single large program, my object oriented code is split apart into several self contained objects.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=93)** Almost like several mini programs where each object contains its own data and logic to describe how it behaves and interacts with other objects.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=103)** The idea here is that we can talk about and use these programmed objects similar to objects in the real world.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=110)** The mixer can mix ingredients together.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=113)** I can pour the mix into the pan and the oven can bake whatever I give it.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=118)** The end result of Barron's procedural approach and my object oriented approach is the same.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=123)** We both made a cake, but the way we thought about the problem and organized our code was very different.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=129)** - Now, neither of these two approaches is better than the other all of the time.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=134)** Each has its own advantages and disadvantages that apply to different situations.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=140)** - One of the main advantages of using an object oriented approach is code re-usability.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=145)** If we want to make something other than a cake, perhaps muffins, I've already created the functionality to mix and bake things in the mixer and the oven.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=154)** So I can reuse those objects.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=157)** I'll need to define one new object to make my muffins, a muffin tray, but I can base the muffin tray on the cake pan object that I already have because they have a lot in common, holding ingredients that go into the oven.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=170)** Realize that object oriented programming is not itself a language.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=175)** Object orientation is referred to as a programming paradigm.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=179)** A set of ideas that's supported by many languages.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=183)** - And there are other programming paradigms beyond just procedural and object orientation.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=188)** If you take a computer science course, you might encounter logic programming languages, like Prolog.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=195)** Or functional programming languages, like Haskell.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=198)** However, those both tend to live in very specialized environments.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=202)** For the practical, pragmatic world of creating web applications, mobile apps, desktop applications, or game development, you'll almost certainly be using object oriented programming languages.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=213)** In fact, all of the top high demand languages today are object oriented.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=218)** Now, many of those languages support multiple paradigms.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=222)** Meaning you can use them to write code in an object oriented way or in a procedural way.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=227)** Your choice.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=229)** When I was first learning object oriented programming in college with Java, I found that most of the small classroom programs I had to write, would have been way quicker and easier to hack together using a procedural approach.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=241)** The amount of object oriented code I had to write to get things working often felt bloated and way beyond the scope of those projects.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/object-oriented-thinking?u=76281980&t=248)** And you may find yourself feeling the same way as you're learning, just keep in mind we use small, simple examples to learn but the real value of object orientation will appear as your projects grow in scale and require changes.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - many (1), - right (1), - now (1), - one (1), - and (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3), find (1)
> **Code Keywords:** self (1), require (1)
> **Analogies:** similar to (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### Objects
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=0)** - The idea behind object-oriented computing is that it makes thinking about and discussing programming similar to thinking about the real world.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=9)** So to ask what's an object in a computer program, start by asking, what's an object in the real world?
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=17)** - So is this cookie an object?
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=20)** - Sure.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=21)** - What about this mug?
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=22)** - Absolutely.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=23)** These are both objects, they're things.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=25)** - What about this mug?
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=27)** Are these two mugs the same object?
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=29)** - No, although they're similar mugs, they're not the same mug.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=34)** These are separate objects, each with its own existence.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=38)** It's own identity that's independent of all other objects.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=42)** Every object has characteristics, inherent properties that describe its current state.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=47)** For example, a mug can be full, empty, or somewhere in between.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=52)** Filling one mug with coffee does not mean all of the mugs in the world get filled.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=56)** The current state of this mug being full is independent of this other empty mug because they're separate objects.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=64)** - But there's more to a mug on whether it's empty or full.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=67)** What about its size?
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=69)** Wait, or color?
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=70)** - Well, most objects will have multiple attributes.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=74)** Some of those attributes like color and size will remain constant.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=78)** Whereas other attributes like whether it's full or empty are likely to change over the mug's lifetime.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=85)** - I usually use the term properties to describe those things.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=88)** - Sure.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=89)** Attributes or properties, characteristics, states, fields, variables, those are all related terms that you'll often hear programmers use interchangeably.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=99)** Their exact meaning may differ slightly depending on the situation and specific context through language but for this course, I'll stick with using the term attributes.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=109)** - But I still prefer properties.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=111)** Okay, so it's easy to think of the mug as an object since it's a single thing.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=117)** But what about something complex like my cellphone which has hundreds of components and electronic parts?
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=123)** - Well, being an object has nothing to do with complexity.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=127)** And it's possible for one object to contain other objects.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=130)** I can talk about your phone as a thing in the real world so I can think of it as a computing object with attributes like color, size, and weight.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=140)** In addition to that, your cellphone has behaviors.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=143)** It can ring, and it can send text messages.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=146)** - But the mug can't ring or text.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=148)** So those behaviors are specific to my cellphone object.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=151)** - Right, and I wouldn't try to use your cellphone to hold coffee.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=155)** Identity, attributes, and behaviors.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=158)** Those are the three things that describe objects in an object-oriented programming language.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=164)** Every object in a computer program is self-contained so it has its own identity separate from other objects, its own attributes to describe its current state and its own behaviors, the things it can do.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=178)** - These examples you've used, mugs, cookies, cellphones, they're all things that we can see and touch.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=184)** But software often describes things that don't have a tangible representation.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=189)** So can objects in a program represent more than physical things?
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=193)** - Good point.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=194)** In programming, the idea of an object extends further to describe non-tangible things too.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=201)** Like a date, a timer, or a bank account.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=204)** You can't touch and hold a bank account in real life but it's still a well-defined idea that meets the definition of an object.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=212)** It has identity, one bank account is separate from another bank account.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=217)** It has attributes like an account number and a balance.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=221)** And it has behaviors, you can deposit to a bank account and withdraw from it.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=226)** One of the challenges of object-oriented design is figuring out if something in your application should be represented as an object.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=235)** That's easy when your application needs something like a mug, a person, or a document.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=240)** But what if you're building an event management application?
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=245)** Would an event be an object?
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=247)** Well, just ask yourself, is it a noun?
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=251)** Nouns aren't just physical things, but also people, places, and ideas or concepts.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=257)** As a simple test, if you were talking about it in a conversation, could you put the word the in front of it?
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=265)** The mug, the person, the document.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=267)** Sure, but also, the bank account, the time, the event.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=272)** Those work too, those could be objects.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=275)** However, you would not say the ringing or the texting.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=280)** Those are verbs that describe the behaviors of an object, not the object itself.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=285)** We need to identify those verbs as well because a program without any behaviors isn't very useful.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/objects?u=76281980&t=292)** But as the name object-oriented implies, when it comes to structuring an object-oriented program, we tend to focus on the nouns first.

#### Classes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=0)** - Objects don't just magically appear in a program.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=4)** We have to create them, and we do so from classes.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=8)** A class is the detailed description, the definition, the template of what an object will be.
>

>

>

>

>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=77)** Now you might encounter other, somewhat interchangeable, terms for these words.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=82)** Instead of name, you might see type, because each class creates objects of a particular type.

> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=88)** And as we talked about earlier, you might also see attributes referred to as properties, or more generically as data.

>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=112)** - What you just described is the textbook definition of a function.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=116)** Code that performs some action and returns a value.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=120)** What's the difference between methods and functions?
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=122)** - Well, methods are basically functions with the key difference that methods are defined as part of a class.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=130)** So they're included in any object in that class.
>

>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=140)** These concepts of classes and objects can be a bit confusing at first, so let's look at an example using our round cookie class.
>

>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=171)** It's just providing a definition.

> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=173)** But, after defining the class, I can create objects based on it through a process called instantiation.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=181)** And it's called that because each object I create is an instance of that particular class.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=187)** I've create two instances here for me and Olivia, but there's really no limit to the number I can create.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=193)** Now, each of the instances, each object has its own identity, independent from other objects.

>

> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=211)** - I think my cookie needs to be a little bit more colorful, so I'm going to put some blue icing on it.

>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=223)** So, to create objects, I need the class first.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=226)** And if I haven't defined the class myself, someone else needs to have already defined it for me.

> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/classes?u=76281980&t=240)** Basic useful things like strings, dates, and arrays are often included as provided classes, so you don't have to begin each program by defining the same common classes over and over again.

#### Abstraction
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=0)** - There are four fundamental ideas in object-oriented programming to keep in mind when creating classes.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=7)** And they have the wonderful names Abstraction.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=10)** Polymorphism.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=11)** Inheritance.

> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=22)** Now, these four words can sound intimidating, but you probably do most of them already in daily thought and conversation, even if you don't use these fancy academic terms to describe them.

>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=44)** I didn't even describe the person.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=46)** Male or female. Tall or short.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=48)** You might have imagined someone in particular. That's OK.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=51)** But I don't have to get that specific because you understand the idea of a person.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=57)** You've known and encountered enough real people to abstract the idea of what a person means.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=63)** Abstraction means we focus on the essential qualities of something rather than one specific example.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=69)** By using abstraction, we automatically discard what's unimportant or irrelevant.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=75)** Your mental model of a person might have a name, a gender, and a height, but it probably doesn't have flavor or icing.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=83)** Those are things that make sense if I was describing a cookie, but they're irrelevant to the idea of a person.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=90)** Abstraction means the idea or concept of a person is completely separate from any specific instance.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=98)** It's what we do all of the time in conversation.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=100)** And it's at the heart of object-oriented programming because it's what we're doing when we make a class.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=106)** I don't create separate classes for each person I need to define in my program.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=111)** I just focus on the essential qualities of the idea I care about and I write one person class.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=119)** I'll focus on things like: each person has a name, a height, and a gender.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=124)** And because I always want to discard what's unimportant, it's never just, what does a person class look like?
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=131)** It's, what should a person class look like for this application?
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=136)** Under these circumstances, at this time.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=138)** Focusing always on just the essentials.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstraction?u=76281980&t=142)** So it might be true that every person has a height, but if my application doesn't care about that piece of information, then I don't need that attribute defined in my class.

#### Encapsulation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=0)** - The idea of encapsulation is about containing the elements of an object.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=5)** Not just to keep them together, but to also protect them.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=9)** - Ow!
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=11)** - We bundle an object's attributes or data along with the methods that operate on that data within the sane unit or the same class.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=19)** One reason for doing that is to restrict access to some of the object's components.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=23)** - Like how you're restricting me from taking a cookie?
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=26)** - No, you can have a cookie, but you'll have to use my method to get to it.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=32)** My cookie jar class has an attribute that represents the number of cookies in the jar.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=37)** When I create an instance of my cookie jar, I don't want you or any other part of my application to be able to reach in and directly change the number of cookies.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=46)** - Oh, come on. You can trust me.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=49)** - It's not only about trust.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=51)** If I let you directly change things, like the number, you might unknowingly change it to something invalid, like a negative one.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=58)** That value doesn't make sense for the number of cookies and might cause my program to crash.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=64)** Another reason to restrict access is that, well, maybe I want to limit the number of cookies you can have.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=70)** - Whoa. No. I don't like the sound of that.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=73)** So what do I have to do to get a cookie?
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=76)** - Well, to control access to that attribute, I'll hide it so it's only accessible from inside the cookie jar object itself.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=85)** Then, I'll add a method called 'request cookie' that can be accessed from other parts of the application.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=91)** When you use the 'request cookie' method, you'll get a cookie, and the method will modify the number of cookies attribute accordingly.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=99)** One of the principles of encapsulation is that an object should not make anything about itself available except what is absolutely necessary for other parts of the application to work.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=111)** This is a concept referred to as 'black boxing'.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=113)** I'm closing off the inner workings of the cookie jar and only revealing specific inputs and outputs.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=120)** You don't need to know how the 'request cookie' method is implemented under the hood to use it.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=125)** - But I'm an engineer. I need to know how everything works.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=130)** - Do you know how your phone works?
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=132)** - Well, sure. I just punch the number into the keypad and it makes a call.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=137)** - Do you know how it works internally?
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=139)** - Well, you see-
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=140)** - It doesn't matter. If you get a new cellphone, the inner workings can be completely different.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=145)** But it has the same buttons to press.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=147)** So you can still use it.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=148)** One of the main benefits with object orientation is that it allows us to more safely change the way the object works without changing the rest of the application.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=159)** Perhaps, when I first wrote my cookie jar class, I represented the total number of cookies as a single value.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=166)** Then, later on, I decide to use three separate values to keep track of each type of cookie individually.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=173)** Since I hid those attributes, I don't have to worry about breaking the other 20 parts of my application that have been using the 'request cookie' method to grab cookies.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=183)** I'll just need to modify the 'request cookie' method and the class to handle the change.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=188)** - Well, if you're the person writing these classes, why would you want to hide your own code?
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=192)** Why keep secrets from yourself?
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=195)** - It's not about being secretive.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=197)** It's about reducing dependencies between different parts of the application.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=201)** The change in one place won't cause a domino effect and require multiple changes elsewhere.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=207)** - Then how much should you hide?
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=209)** - Well, different languages have different levels of support for hiding properties and methods.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/encapsulation?u=76281980&t=214)** But the general rule is to encapsulate as much as possible.

#### Inheritance
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=0)** When I need to create a new class it may not always be necessary to build it from scratch.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=5)** If my program has an existing class that's similar to what I need, then I can use inheritance to base my new class on that existing one.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=14)** Inheritance enables a new class to receive or inherit the attributes and methods of existing classes using the same implementation which is a great form of code reuse.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=26)** Let's say for example I want to model the characters present in a bakery.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=31)** I might start by defining a class for customers, which has attributes for the customer's name, phone number, email address, and a customer ID number.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=40)** Additionally I give that customer class a method to update their contact information, and a method to purchase items.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=50)** The bakery also has employees so I create an employee class with attributes for the employee's name, phone number, email address, and employee ID number.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=60)** The employee can update their contact information, get promoted, or retire.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=65)** Now, looking at these two classes side by side, you might have noticed that they have quite a few similarities.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=72)** Employee's and customer's both have a name, phone number, email address, and a method to update their contact information.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=79)** Which are things that are common to all people.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=82)** So rather then defining those attributes separately in each class, I can create a person class with all of those common attributes and methods.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=92)** And then base the customer and employee classes on it.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=95)** The phrase to use here is customer inherits from person.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=100)** Which means the new customer class automatically gets everything in the person class.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=105)** All of it's attributes and behavior's without having to write any extra code.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=110)** From there, I can add any additional attributes, and methods that are unique to customer's and employee's.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=116)** By convention if I'm drawing a class diagram with inheritance I'll use this style of an arrow to show the relationship between these two classes, and indicate it's direction.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=128)** The term that's most commonly used for this relationship is that the person class is the super class, and the new customer and employee classes are subclasses of it.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=138)** You'll also hear these described as the parent class, and the child class, which is typically used when there's only a single level of inheritance, or the base class and the derived class.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=150)** Which better captures the concept of inheritance that's multiple levels deep.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=154)** That said there's no limit to the number of child classes I can create based off of a single parent.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=161)** If I later realize my shop needs another class for the courier that delivers packages, that's easy to add.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=168)** I'll just create the courier as a subclass of person, and give it a unique method to deliver packages.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=175)** In addition to benefiting from code reuse, the great thing about inheritance is that if I make a change in a super class, it will automatically filter down and effect all of the subclasses.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=187)** So when I realized I should have been keeping track of each person's email address all along, because it's the twenty first century, I only need to add that attribute to the person class, and then the customer, employee, and courier classes will all get the email attributes too.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=204)** Changing code in one place is much easier then having to change it in three.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=209)** Now a few languages like Python, and C++, allow you to inherit from more then one super class.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=216)** Bringing in attributes and behaviors from multiple other classes.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=220)** However, multiple inheritance can get confusing so it's much more common to see single inheritance where a subclass only inherits from one parent or superclass.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/inheritance?u=76281980&t=230)** Languages like Java, C#, Swift, and Ruby enforce single inheritance with classes and that's what we'll be using in this course.

> **CLI Commands:** make (1), python (1), ruby (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### Polymorphism
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=0)** - Our fourth and final object-oriented concept is polymorphism which is a complicated sounding word that simply means having many forms.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=10)** And appropriately enough, there are multiple forms of polymorphism.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=15)** One form, called dynamic or run-time polymorphism, allows us to access methods using the same interface on different types of objects that may implement those methods in different ways.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=27)** Think of this basic coffee maker as a class with a method to brew coffee.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=32)** The input parameters for that brew method are a scoop of ground coffee beans and water.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=39)** I wait a bit for it to brew and the output is a fresh cup of hot coffee.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=45)** - I prefer to use a different type of coffee maker.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=48)** A French press.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=49)** My French press also has a brew method with the exact same inputs and outputs as Baron's basic coffee maker.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=56)** I input a scoop of ground coffee and some water.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=61)** Then after a little while, I press down the plunger and the output is a fresh cup of hot coffee.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=69)** - Now, although these two coffee makers have the exact same type of inputs and outputs, the way that they brew is very different.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=78)** My coffee maker uses a filter whereas Olivia's French press strains the coffee with a metal screen.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=84)** - And although the outputs we received are the same type of object, a cup of coffee, their attributes may have some different values.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=91)** When it comes to taste, my French press coffee is better.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=95)** - Now, when it comes to actually implementing this type of polymorphism in code, there are several ways it could be done.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=101)** Olivia's fancy FrenchPress could inherit all of the attributes and methods from my BasicCoffeeMaker.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=108)** But then, it replaces the brew method it inherited from the BasicCoffeeMaker through a process called overriding.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=115)** That allows the FrenchPress to redefine its own unique version of the brew method.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=120)** - Or perhaps both of these coffee makers inherit from the same abstract class with an abstract brew method.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=126)** - Or they both agree to implement the same interface.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=130)** Inheritance, abstract classes, and interfaces are all possible implementations of polymorphism that we'll cover later on.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=138)** The benefit of dynamic polymorphism here is that it enables me to use any form of coffee maker as long as it has a brew method that takes coffee grounds and water as inputs and returns a cup of coffee.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=150)** - Even a really fancy coffee maker like this one?
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=152)** - Well, I've never seen one of those before, but if it has that brew method, I can use it.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=159)** The other common form of polymorphism is called static or compile-time polymorphism.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=164)** And it uses a feature of many object-oriented programming languages called method overloading, not to be confused with method overriding.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=173)** Overloading allows you to implement multiple methods within a class that have the same name, but a different set of input parameters.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=182)** If I give the brew method of the French press, coffee and water as input arguments, I'll get back a cup of coffee.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=188)** But if I change the type of inputs, from coffee and water to tea leaves and water, that's a different set of parameters so the French press will execute a different version of the brew method that gives me back a cup of hot tea.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=202)** Brew with coffee and water and brew with tea and water are two different methods with two different signatures.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=209)** And the FrenchPress will automatically execute the appropriate one depending on what you give it.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=215)** - [Olivia] You can also have variations of the method with different numbers of input parameters.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=220)** I could implement a version of the brew method that takes three inputs, coffee and tea, and water.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=226)** - You could do that, but I'm not sure you'd want to drink what you get out.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=230)** In most cases, those overloaded methods will provide different, but very similar functionality.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=236)** So those are the different forms of polymorphism.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=239)** Defining different classes that can be used with the same interface.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/polymorphism?u=76281980&t=243)** - Or overloading a method from the same class that can take different sets of parameters.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** brew (15)
> **Speakers:** - or (3), - now (2), - our (1), - i (1), - and (1)
> **Code Keywords:** interface (3), abstract (3), while, (1), static (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), is called (1)

#### Analysis, design, and programming
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=0)** - When you see the words object-oriented, there's usually another word right beside it, object oriented programming, object oriented design, or object oriented analysis.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=12)** These are all connected and refer to the idea that to develop any piece of software, you need to do three things, understand your problem, plan your solution, and finally, build it, or rather, analysis, design, and programming.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=28)** Although they're listed as two separate steps, analysis and design are usually talked about together because they encompass everything that should happen before you write a single line of code.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=38)** Analysis answers the question what do you need to do?
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=42)** What's the problem you're trying to solve?
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=44)** And design figures out how you're going to do it.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=47)** In this course, we'll go through an entire object oriented analysis and design process to produce the deliverables, the conceptual design that you, or maybe a separate team of programmers, can take and use to build a solution.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=60)** - Conceptual is the key word here.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=63)** We won't be writing any code.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=64)** - What about drawing diagrams?
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=66)** - Sure.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=67)** - Sketches on a whiteboard?
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=68)** - Those are great.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=69)** - Written descriptions?
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=70)** - Absolutely, but no code.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=73)** Now, there are lots of formal methodologies with their own unique name and conventions, but the ideas are all fairly similar.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=81)** For this course, we'll follow a typical five-step approach.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=84)** Start by gathering requirements to figure out what our application needs to do.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=89)** Really flesh out the problem you're trying to solve.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=92)** After that, describe the application.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=95)** Build a narrative in plain, conversational language for how people will use it.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=100)** Step three involves identifying the most important objects, which is the starting point for identifying actual classes.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=108)** Once those objects have been identified, formally describe the interactions between them, understanding each object's responsibilities, the behaviors they need to have, and when they interact with other objects.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=122)** And finally, create a class diagram, which serves as the main output from the five-step process.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=128)** The class diagram is a visual representation of the classes in the application, and creating it is where the object oriented principles like inheritance and polymorphism really come into play.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/analysis-design-and-programming?u=76281980&t=140)** We'll be diving into each of these steps in more detail, and we'll cover different techniques that you can use for each one.
