#### Unified modeling language (UML)
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=0)** - Throughout this course, we'll be using a few common diagramming techniques for drawing classes and their interactions.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=7)** These diagrams are from something called UML or the Unified Modeling Language.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=14)** UML is not a programming language.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=17)** It's a graphical notation for drawing diagrams to visualize object oriented systems.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=23)** For example, The class diagram gives a very simple graphical representation of a class.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=30)** It has three sections; the name of the class, its attributes or fields and its behaviors or methods.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=37)** It allows me to quickly sketch out an idea that's readable and understandable regardless of which programming language I'll be using.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=45)** UML includes over a dozen different types of structural and behavioral diagrams.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=50)** Now you should never be asking the question where can I write some sequence diagrams?
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=56)** Instead, you should simply realize that one would come in handy when thinking about or discussing a situation that isn't clear.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=64)** For this course, we'll only be using a few of the most common types of diagrams including class diagrams and use case diagrams which portray a users interaction with the program.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=75)** UML is not the goal here and knowing more UML will not necessarily make you a better object oriented developer.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=83)** In fact, knowing a little UML may actually be more useful than knowing a lot of UML because knowing a lot of UML can lead to an over emphasis on the diagrams themselves.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=95)** These diagrams should be a quick, useful communication tool.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=99)** A support system for your brain, not the other way around.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=102)** I'm a big fan of doing my diagramming work on paper or a whiteboard at least in the initial stages of a project.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=109)** With that said as a project matures and grows in size, it can be useful to capture those UML diagrams in an electronic format to facilitate sharing amongst team members.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=120)** There are a lot of tools to choose from here with commercial and open sourse options across every platform.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=127)** Their capabilities range from simple drawing tools to help you build diagrams, to more advanced tools that can generate code from UML class diagrams.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=136)** I'm not going to make any particular recommendations here.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=139)** The single best source for finding out about available UML tools is actually the Wikipedia page List of Unified Modeling Language tools which will allow you to compare tools to see whether or not they're open source, which platforms they run on and the different languages they can generate code for.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=158)** If you decide that you want to dive deeper into UML then I highly recommend UML Distilled by Martin Fowler.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/unified-modeling-language-uml?u=76281980&t=165)** It's a great book, short book, all the UML that 99.9% of developers on the planet would ever need to know.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** uml (14)
> **Code Keywords:** class. (1), class, (1), for. (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Versions:** 99.9 (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Best Practices:** you should never (1)
> **Speakers:** - throughout (1)


### 2. Requirements

#### Defining requirements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=1)** - The first step to any design process is to gather your requirements.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=5)** Figure out what your application or product needs to do.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=9)** What's the problem you're trying to solve?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=11)** Why are you building the application in the first place?
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=15)** At the core what are the application's functional requirements?
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=18)** Literally, what does it need to do?
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=20)** What are the necessary features and capabilities?
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=24)** In addition to those, there may also be non-functional requirements that place constraints on how the application should function.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=31)** For example, an application that handles banking transactions, or one that stores health care data, will likely have to comply with certain regulations.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=41)** There could be performance requirements such as response time, or how many users can be supported simultaneously.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=48)** Support requirements, if there's an issue with the application at two A.M. on a Sunday morning, what needs to happen?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=55)** And security can be considered as either a non-functional or functional requirement depending on the application.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=62)** From my own experiences, one industry that loves to write laws of detailed requirements is the aerospace industry.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=69)** - If you're going to put something into space, you need a solid set of requirements to make sure it works right the first time.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=75)** - So, as a pair of daring aerospace engineers, let's write some requirements together.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=80)** - We've been tasked to create a space microwave to go onboard our lunar space ship.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=85)** So the astronauts can have nice warm meals on their way to the moon.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=89)** Like many projects, creating a space microwave will involve both software and hardware elements but the process of writing requirements is a general skill that applies to all types of projects.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=101)** - Normally if you're developing an application for a customer or client, you'll want to get as much information to understand the problem they want to solve.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=110)** Now that client may have only a vague idea of what they need or they think they know exactly what they need, and try to hand you a finished set of requirements.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=120)** As the developer, always take the time to understand why the client wants to do something to make sure you can give them what they really need.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=128)** - Well, that sounds really wise, Olivia, but I'm the master of microwave meals, so I'm pretty sure I know all the bells and whistles our space microwave needs.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=137)** - Bern, I trust you have amazing ideas, but a common trap many developers fall into is having a dozen semi formed ideas about the cool features their app could have, versus nailing down what it really needs to do.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=151)** If you're the driving force behind an app, and it's your idea, it's easy to think that you've thought through it all.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=157)** You already know what you want, and you skip this step.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=161)** Let's focus together, be specific, and write things down.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=165)** - Fear not, I'm two steps ahead of you.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=169)** To write the functional requirements, I use the phrase, the system must, or the application must, to describe what it must do.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=177)** So, the system must heat food in space packages, allow the user to set a time for the food to be ready, notify the user when their food is ready, via space pager, of course, and the system must set the cooking time based on the food it's given.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=197)** These requirements can be short and simple statements, or they could be something more complicated, as long as it's specific.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=204)** - Those look pretty good, except for this fifth one.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=207)** That the application must inherit food types from the abstract super class.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=212)** Scratch that from the list.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=214)** What we're doing here, defining requirements, has nothing to do with object orientation.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=219)** The requirements shouldn't contain words like inheritance, or abstraction, or even class, or object.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=225)** Those are details that we'll get to later.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=228)** So, have you thought about non-functional requirements yet?
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=231)** - Of course, they're on the back.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=235)** Since these describe required characteristics of the application, rather than features, I phrased the non-functional requirements to describe how the application should be.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=245)** They describe the ilities, like maintainability, reliability, usability, and availability.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=253)** - [Female Developer] So our system should be available 24/7?
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=256)** - Astronauts are always hungry.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=258)** - It should be usable while wearing work gloves.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=260)** And it should be compatible with Windows, Mac, Linx, hold on a second.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=265)** That last requirement is a bit excessive.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=267)** Does a microwave really need to be compatible with all those platforms?
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=271)** - Well, not yet, but including that requirement to make sure it's ready for everything.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=278)** - And that's a common mistake.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=279)** You don't need to be exhaustive and have answers for everything up front.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=283)** On the first pass, only focus on capturing the absolute minimum set of requirements.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=289)** Not the things are optional, or nice to have, or your dream features.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=293)** Just the bare necessities, your minimum viable product.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=297)** If the first pass at requirements isn't perfect, that's okay, you can update the requirements later if needed.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/defining-requirements?u=76281980&t=303)** The goal at this point is to get something written down and that could be as simple as bullet point son paper.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - well (2), - the (1), - if (1), - so (1), - we (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), pass (2), function (1), abstract (1), super (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1)
> **Warnings:** common mistake (1)

#### FURPS+ requirements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One commonly used model for classifying software quality attributes is the acronym FURPS which stands for functionality, usability, reliability, performance and supportability.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=13)** FURPS serves as a checklist of several key qualities to consider when determining requirements.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=21)** Functionality refers to the capabilities and features of the app.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=25)** This is a core of what the customer wants.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=29)** Usability affects the person who will be using the program.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=32)** Is it easy on the eyes?
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=34)** Is it intuitive to use?
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=36)** Is the documentation accurate and complete?
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=39)** To define reliability requirements, you'll need to know how much system downtime is acceptable.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=45)** If the failures are predictable and how the system can be recovered.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=50)** Performance requirements dictate the application's response time through put.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=54)** And they put limits on the system resources it can use.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=58)** In supportability.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=59)** Make sure the application can be tested, extended, serviced and installed and configured.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=66)** Now in addition to those five categories there is an extension to the FURPS model called FURPS plus which add four more categories.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=76)** Design addresses constraints on how the software must be built because the app requires certain things such as a relational database.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=84)** Implementation.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=86)** Does it have to be written in a certain language?
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=88)** Are there standards or methodologies that need to be followed?
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=92)** The interface typically refers not to the user interface but to an external system that needs to be interfaced with.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=99)** And finally, physical requirements.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=102)** Actual physical constraints related to the hardware the application will be deployed on.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=108)** Keep in mind that the purpose of FURPS plus is to prompt you to think about certain key requirement areas.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=113)** But not all of these will be relevant to every project.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=117)** As you go through the list, it's perfectly acceptable to have not applicable or to be determined for some of these categories.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=125)** Now, requirements analysis is an entire discipline that goes way beyond what we covered in this course.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=132)** If you need to dive deeper, there are lots of great books on the subject that provide formal processes that you can follow.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=139)** Software Requirements by Karl Wiegers and Mastering the Requirements Process by Suzanne and James Robertson are great references.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/furps-plus-requirements?u=76281980&t=147)** If you need to elicit requirements from a customer, particularly in an area you're not familiar with, these are great books and provide some good structures for doing so.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** furps (5)
> **Definitions:** is an  (2), stands for (1), refers to (1), is a  (1)
> **Code Keywords:** interface (2), finally, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Cross-References:** we covered (1)
> **Documentation:** the documentation (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### Challenge: Jukebox requirements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=0)** - All right folks, it's time for a challenge to practice defining requirements.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=4)** Our astronauts need some music for their long journey to the moon.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=8)** So we need your help to design a juke box for our moon shuttle.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=11)** - Now astronauts don't carry money with them, so this juke box will be free to use.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=16)** - But we don't want to let just one person pick all the music and drive the others crazy, like our last road trip together.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=24)** - I can't help it, I love disco.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=27)** - [Female Scientist]The juke box should let a user select an album from the available music library, and then choose individual songs from that album to be played.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=36)** And as an extra precaution, if one astronaut adds more than three songs in a row to the play queue, and another astronaut wants to play a song, they'll jump ahead in line.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=47)** Now, your goal for this challenge is to write a set of requirements to design the juke box.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=53)** Try to come up with at least three functional and three non-functional requirements.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=57)** But you can certainly write more for extra practice.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=61)** If you find yourself struggling with where to start on this challenge, use the attributes of FURPS+ to inspire possible requirements.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=68)** - Now we've intentionally left the details somewhat vague here, because this challenge is about the process of thinking through a new, often half-formed idea to discover and actually write down requirements.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=82)** There's no one correct answer to this challenge, so take some time to think it through, and when you're done, be sure to watch our solution video to see how we approached the problem.

#### Solution: Jukebox requirements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=4)** - To work our way towards a solution for the challenge of defining jukebox requirements, we'll use the FIRBS acronym as a guide.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=11)** "F" stands for Functionality.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=13)** So, we'll start with functional requirements, the things the system must do.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=18)** Some of the core functions of a jukebox, or pretty much any music player, include maintaining a music library of albums and songs, allowing users to browse those albums and songs, and, in our case, allowing users to select individual songs, and preventing them from playing full albums.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=37)** They will also need to maintain a queue of songs to play.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=41)** - Let's not forget, the most important functionality.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=44)** The jukebox needs to actually play music.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=47)** It'd be a pretty lousy jukebox, otherwise.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=49)** You know, in addition to letting the user browse by album, I think we should include a button that they can click to sort the songs by artist.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=58)** - That's a good idea, but it should be phrased differently as a requirement.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=63)** Saying that the system must have a button that the user can click to sort by artist, is too implementation-focused.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=70)** A better way to capture that requirement, would be to say, the system must allow the user to sort by artist, because it leaves the implementation open ended.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=81)** Now, for a few more functional requirements that are more specific to our scenario.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=87)** Our jukebox will need to identify individual users, and track the number of plays per user, to prevent one astronaut from hogging the controls.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=97)** Notice that we haven't specified how the system will identify the users.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=101)** The requirement just states that it must do it.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=104)** - There are several ways we could accomplish that task.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=106)** Maybe, we give each user a unique ID to type in before they use the system, or we could use a fingerprint reader, or perhaps a smart camera to identify the user's face.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=117)** At this point, we're not committed to a specific way of accomplishing the identification requirement.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=123)** So, we'll save those implementation details for a later stage in the design process.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=128)** Now, let's move on to the nonfunctional requirements, things the system should be.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=133)** These are where the remainder of the FIRBS attributes will likely contribute.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=137)** In regards to its usability, the system should be intuitive to use while the astronauts are floating around in space.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=144)** Maybe that means it'll have really big buttons or voice commands.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=148)** We don't know, yet.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=150)** Thinking about reliability, astronauts could need tunes at anytime.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=155)** So, we'll want the jukebox to be available for them all day, everyday, 24/7.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=161)** - Our jukebox shouldn't require so much power to operate.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=164)** That impacts other critical systems aboard the spacecraft.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=167)** So, a good performance requirement would be for the system to be low-power.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=172)** - And, we'll certainly need to define a threshold for what low-power actually means.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=177)** - And finally, to address supportability.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=180)** I think the jukebox should have an updatable music library, so that astronauts can have some modern song choices.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=188)** Now, the informal requirements we came up with here are, by no means, a complete set.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=194)** They're just starting points.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=195)** If you create your own list of requirements, it might have a lot in common with ours, or it can be wildly different.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=201)** There's no one right answer, here.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-requirements?u=76281980&t=204)** The goal is to get comfortable thinking about and writing down requirements.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - and (2), - to (1), - let (1), - that (1), - there (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), case, (1), require (1), finally, (1)
> **Env Vars:** firbs (2)
> **Definitions:** stands for (1)


### 3. Use Cases and User Stories

#### Use cases
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=1)** - After hashing out the feature focused requirements that say what the system or application must do, it's time to shift focus towards the user and how they accomplish a particular goal, and one way to capture that is as a use case.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=15)** There isn't a single right way to write use cases, and they could be written at several levels of formality.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=22)** But at the very least, a use case needs three essential things: a title that describes a goal, the person who will interact with the application to achieve that goal, referred to as a primary actor, and the execution flow, or steps needed to accomplish the goal, the success scenario here.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=41)** The title for a use case should be a short phrase with an active verb that describes a goal.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=46)** - Well, as an astronaut, my first goal would be to heat up a delicious meal package.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=52)** - A title for that use case could simply be Heat Meal.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=56)** - What if I want to set a timer to heat that meal package later so it'll be ready when I return from my space walk?
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=62)** - I'd call that Heat Delayed Meal.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=64)** We want to keep titles short and simple.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=67)** Both of those examples could be separate use cases, separate distinct goals for the application.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=73)** Now, in regards to the actor, the reason that we say actor rather than generic user is because we often need to identify exactly who is having this interaction.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=84)** It could be a user, but it could also be someone more specific, customer, employee, or administrator.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=90)** - So the actors are all of the humans that could interact with and use the program.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=94)** - Not just humans, any external entity that acts on the system is an actor, including other computer systems.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=101)** Now, the real meat of each use case lies in the scenario, the details for accomplishing the goal.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=108)** One way to write the scenario is as a single paragraph, like this one for the Heat Meal use case.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=114)** It starts with the astronaut inserting the meal package, the system identifying the package, heating it up for the correct amount of time, notifying the astronaut that it's ready via space pager, and finally, the astronaut returning to remove the hot meal package.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=131)** The aim is to write this so it's short and succinct, using everyday, non-technical language so it can be understood by a typical user of the application.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=141)** Another way to format this scenario is as a numbered list of individual steps.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=145)** So, step one, the astronaut inserts the meal package.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=150)** Step two, the system identifies the type of meal, and so on.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=154)** There's not a hard and fast way to write these.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=157)** I can use different phrasing or different levels of detail.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=161)** Really, it's whatever makes sense for the situation.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=164)** - That example describes a normal successful scenario where everything works as expected, but I know if something can go wrong, it will.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=173)** So how do you capture that in a use case?
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=176)** - Well, use cases are typically written to describe successful operations.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=181)** But depending on the situation, you can also add extensions or steps for alternative flows, for what happens when things go wrong, like when the system can't identify the meal package, or it has trouble accessing the space pager system.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=197)** And if it's more useful, you can add other specific details about the scenario.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=201)** One common example would be a precondition that must be true to begin the use case.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=207)** Here, to heat a meal, our precondition would be that an astronaut has identified at least one package to cook.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=213)** That's the only way this use case makes sense.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=216)** Now, you can take it all the way to what's called a fully dressed use case by adding even more fields for post-conditions, secondary actors, stakeholders, and so on.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=227)** These often exist as a PDF or Word template that you can fill in.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=231)** - That just went from simple to complicated real quick.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=234)** - And that's something to watch out for.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=237)** Formality often sounds like a good idea, but requiring that level of detail can kill progress.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=243)** If you're managing a large, formal project with team members spread across the globe, then maybe you need fully dressed use cases.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=251)** But for most projects, it's much better to have a set of readable, casual use cases than a collection of super formal, multi-page templates that never get completed.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=261)** - It sounds like you could easily get bogged down writing use cases.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=265)** - As a rule of thumb, you shouldn't spend more than a few days working on use cases in any iteration of a project.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=271)** Get 'em done so you can move forward onto the next phase.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=275)** You may have to make some assumptions if you don't have all the information at this point, and that's okay.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=281)** The use cases may evolve or change.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/use-cases?u=76281980&t=283)** If you're interested in diving deeper into the world of use cases, Alistair Cockburn's Writing Effective Use Cases is the book on the subject, with terrific examples, common mistakes to watch out for, and discussions on writing style.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - well (2), - that (2), - after (1), - a (1), - what (1)
> **Code Keywords:** case. (3), finally, (1), for. (1), super (1), for, (1)
> **Warnings:** watch out (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Env Vars:** pdf (1)
> **Exercise Files:** template (1)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1)
> **Best Practices:** rule of thumb (1)

#### Identifying the actors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=1)** - Before you dive headfirst into writing use cases, it's good to spend a few minutes brainstorming possible actors.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=8)** You're looking for anything that lives outside of your application, but interacts with it to accomplish some goal.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=14)** - Sometimes identifying the actors is straightforward.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=17)** If we were building a simple single player video game, then there could be just one actor, the player.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=24)** - However, something like the space microwave that we're designing could have multiple people interacting with it to accomplish different goals.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=32)** Thinking about their different job titles or departments can also prompt ideas for use case scenarios.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=38)** As we're brainstorming, we'll use stick figures to represent each of the possible human actors for our application.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=45)** - [Man] Well, the spaceship will have several astronauts on board that need to use the space microwave to eat, the mission commander, the pilot, and the flight engineer, and back on Earth, Mission Control will want to monitor the status of the equipment, and I'm sure the astronauts will have nutritionists keeping an eye on what they eat.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=65)** - Now, in addition to identifying people, you should also ask does the application need to interact with other computer systems or other organizations?
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=74)** Those external systems are considered actors too.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=77)** - Since we have a requirement to notify astronauts when their meal is ready via space pager, the microwave will need to interface with some sort of space pager system.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=87)** But we don't know what that system will be yet, or what the interface to it will even look like.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=92)** - And that's okay.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=93)** For now, all we're doing is identifying actors as a starting point, which should be painless and straightforward.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=100)** We can represent that external pager service, whatever it ends up being, as a box over on the right side.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=106)** I will draw a line to show that it interfaces with our system.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=110)** As we get further along in the implementation details, we may learn that this one box should actually be several different external systems that the microwave needs to interface with.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=120)** And if so, we'll just update our collection of actors and use cases accordingly.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=126)** Now, looking at our list, most of the human actors we've identified are all based on roles or job titles.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=133)** But keep in mind that the same person with the same role and job title could actually be different actors at different times.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=140)** The focus should really be on the goal that the actor wants to accomplish, and how we define those actors can vary depending on the use case.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=148)** For example, in a use case for heating meals, I would expect all of the different astronauts on the spaceship to use the system in the same way, to prepare a package of food.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=159)** And the people back on Earth, Mission Control and the nutritionist, are simply monitoring that activity.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=165)** In this scenario, there isn't a difference between the on-board astronauts.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=169)** They all have the same goal.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=171)** So this situation really comes down to somebody preparing a meal, and someone monitoring it.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=177)** So, it might make sense to phrase the actors for this use case as the cook and the monitor, not a job title or a specific role, but a perfectly acceptable name for the actors who take part in this particular use case.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=191)** - Hold on a sec.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=192)** Use cases always have a single primary actor and possibly one or more secondary actors, if required, so who's the primary here?
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=201)** The cook or the monitor?
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=203)** - Well, when writing use cases, the primary actors aren't necessarily the most important actor in the scenario.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-actors?u=76281980&t=210)** They're just the one who initiated it, so in this situation the primary actor would be the cook, and the monitors would be the secondary actors.

> **Code Keywords:** interface (3), case. (2)

> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### Identifying the scenarios
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=1)** - When writing use case scenarios describe a goal that an actor can accomplish in a single encounter and stay focused on the user's intention, what they really want to accomplish.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=11)** - So, something like turning on the microwave.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=14)** - Well, at first, turn on microwave might sound like a good use case.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=18)** After all, it has an active verb phrase and it could have multiple steps and conditions.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=25)** But if we emphasize the user's intent, their goal, their goal isn't to turn on the microwave.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=31)** The reason they want to turn it on is to do something.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=34)** So what is that something?
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=35)** - Well, they probably just want to cook a meal.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=38)** - Bingo!
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=39)** Cook meal, generate reports, change settings, order supplies.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=43)** These are user focused goals.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=45)** Each with several steps that can be accomplished in one encounter.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=49)** Turning on the microwave might be a part of one of these use cases, but it's not a use case by itself.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=55)** On the other side of the spectrum, a goal on the level of feed entire crew or execute mission might be too broad.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=63)** Those would involve multiple encounters with whatever application is being used.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=67)** - But I've seen some pretty broad use cases from developers.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=71)** - True, some people define these broader and smaller use cases as a way of tying things together.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=77)** But at least initially, you want to focus on the use cases that capture the user's true goal.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=82)** Emphasizing the goal of one encounter.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=85)** Now a simple casual use case can have multiple scenarios.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=89)** You should focus on the main successful scenario, the sunny day use case when everything goes right, but when necessary you can describe the alternate paths or extensions.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=99)** - Like, when the microwave can't identify the meal packet that's put in it.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=104)** - Yep.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=105)** - Or when the astronaut tries to shove too many meal packets in all at once.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=109)** - Bingo!
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=109)** - Like when a random cosmic ray strikes the microwave and heats the food packages too fast and they all explode.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=116)** - Too far.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=118)** You're not trying to envision all the bizarrely, unlikely, but technically possible events.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=124)** Just focus on the typical situation that would occur, what you'd want to do with those situations.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=130)** Now you could write these as paragraphs or numbered steps.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=133)** The goal is readability and ease of creation over formality.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=137)** When you're writing, use an active voice.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=140)** Omit needless words and needless detail.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=143)** It's very common to see sentences phrased like, "the system is provided" "with the meal package by the astronaut," but you could just as easily say, "astronaut inserts meal package."
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=154)** Active voice, easy to read, short and concise.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=158)** Another common thing you may see, particularly from programmers, is too much detail or the start of describing the implementation.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=165)** So, "the system connects to the external pager system" "over HTTPS and uses JSON to format the text message" "to be transmitted, then waits for" "a delegated callback response."
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=176)** The goal here isn't to write pseudo-code.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=179)** That's way too much.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=180)** For a use case, "system sends pager message," would be perfectly fine.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=186)** Another thing that's tempting to describe, but doesn't belong in use cases, is the user interface.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=192)** You should describe the scenario without using words like screen, click, button, select.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=198)** None of that.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=199)** There's no, "User pressed the start button."
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=201)** Focus on the intention, the layout of the user interface will follow the function of the application and what it needs to do.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=209)** - After you've come up with your first set of use cases, how do you know if you've missed anything?
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=214)** - Well, you can never know for sure, but questions that may prompt a new goal or actor are: Who does system administration?
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=222)** If this is a system that needs to be started and stopped, or backed up on weekends, or have software updates applied, who does that and how do they interact with the application?
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=232)** Who manages users and security?
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=234)** Particularly if you have role based actors.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=237)** What happens if the system fails?
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=239)** Well, the person who reacts to this may not be a classic user, they're certainly an actor.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=245)** And is anyone looking at performance metrics or system activity logs?
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-scenarios?u=76281980&t=250)** These questions will often prompt a couple of fairly obvious actors for the application, particularly if it's being developed for internal use.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - well (3), - bingo (2), - like (2), - when (1), - so (1)
> **Code Keywords:** interface (2), case. (1), case, (1), function (1)
> **Env Vars:** https (1), json (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### Diagramming use cases
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=1)** - The use case diagram is another diagram that comes from UML, but the name can be a bit misleading.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=7)** It sounds like a diagram of a use case, but in actuality, it's a diagram of several use cases and multiple actors at the same time.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=15)** It shows the relationship between the actors and the different use cases in which they're involved.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=21)** Now, this is not a substitution for written use cases.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=24)** We definitely still need those.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=26)** Use case diagrams and written use cases are two distinct products that show different perspectives and compliment one another.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=33)** - We've finished brainstorming the actors and wrote a few casual use cases, so I think we're ready to draw our first use case diagram.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=41)** - Agreed. And since I can't draw while I'm talking, I'm giving you the markers.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=47)** Start by listing out the use cases.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=49)** Heat meal, generate reports, change settings.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=53)** These aren't in any particular order, and I'm not trying to be exhaustive, just listing a few use cases that make sense.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=61)** For the actors, let's say they're all role based.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=64)** And for now, we're keeping it simple with just an astronaut and their nutritionist back on earth.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=70)** And since those are all human actors, we'll represent them as stick figures.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=75)** Stick figures are perfect cause they're easy.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=78)** Easy on paper, easy on the white board.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=80)** This doesn't need to look impressive.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=82)** And just as we show actors with stick figures, draw elipses around the use case titles to make it obvious they're self contained use cases and not just a piece of text.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=93)** Now draw a box around all the use cases to represent the boundaries of the system.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=98)** Anything inside of the box is part of the application we're trying to write.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=102)** And everything that's not, is outside.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=106)** Next, draw a line between any of the actors and the use cases they will interact with.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=110)** An astronaut can heat up a meal, so draw a line from the astronaut to that use case.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=116)** The astronaut might want to generate a report to see what they've been eating, and their nutritionist will want to do the same.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=124)** And finally, the nutritionist may want to tweak the microwave cook settings to make sure the astronauts are getting the best meals possible.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=132)** These lines don't need arrows because they're not about direction.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=136)** They're merely saying which actors use which use case.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=140)** Now, part of the use case to heat a meal needs to interface with a separate external space pager system to notify the astronaut when their food is ready.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=150)** We could use a stick figure, but since it's a nonhuman actor, I prefer to use a box instead.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=157)** Draw it over on the right side and write the word actor with two angle quotes to make it obvious what it is.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=165)** And finally, connect the line between the external system and the heat meal use case that interacts with it.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=172)** It's common to put the primary actors on the left hand side, those are the ones that initiate any of the use cases, and the secondary actors on the right hand side, the actors that take more of a reactive role, but even then, that's not a hard and fast rule.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=188)** Also, this diagram is not referring to a specific sequence that the use cases need to be executed in, so there's no order that they need to be listed.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=198)** It's meant to be a simple overview of the relationships between multiple use cases and multiple actors at the same time, without the details of each particular written use case.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=208)** It can be useful as a communication tool, even with business users, because it's not particularly technical.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/diagramming-use-cases?u=76281980&t=214)** It can be helpful to figure out if something is missing from the picture.
