#### User stories
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=1)** - User stories are another written format for describing parts of an application, but they're shorter and simpler than a use case.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=8)** A user story still describes a single small scenario from a user's perspective, focusing on their goal, what they want to do and why rather than focusing on the system.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=20)** But unlike a use case, which can be several pages, a user story is typically written as just one, perhaps two, sentences and they're very commonly written on index cards because that forces us to keep them short and sweet.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=34)** Even though they're concise, user stories generally follow a format that looks something like this.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=40)** As a, type of user or role, I want, here you describe the goal, so that, the reason or the benefit.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=48)** The final part, the so that, is optional, but it's very useful to provide context on how this application will be used.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=57)** - Here's one for you.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=59)** As an astronaut, I want to heat up my food so that I can eat a warm meal.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=64)** - Great!
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=65)** It focuses on one specific goal of one specific user for a particular reason.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=70)** The idea is that you can quickly brainstorm a lot of user stories, even unique ones.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=75)** - How' about, as a nutritionist I want to see what astronauts eat so that I can monitor their diet.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=82)** - Perfect!
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=83)** Let's make the nutritionist's job easier.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=86)** Just as when writing use cases, the focus is on intent and should not include descriptions of the user interface.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=93)** - So, "As an astronaut, I want to press a button" "to delay when my food gets cooked," "so that it will be ready later," would be a bad user story?
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=103)** - Right.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=104)** Leave the user interface out of it.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=106)** It would be better to say, "As an astronaut, I want to schedule when" "to heat my food, so it will be ready later."
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=113)** That leaves flexibility for how we implement it.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=116)** Also, avoid detailing alternate paths or exceptions, or listing any technical information in user stories.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=124)** These are intended to be quick, readable summaries of a specific goal and why the user wants it.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=130)** They can be done very early on, often right at the start of a project and they serve as a placeholder for deeper conversations that you need to have.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=140)** - These user stories sound an awful lot like they're just short use cases.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=144)** - It can seem like that at first, but use cases and user stories really are very different things.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=151)** The most obvious difference is the format, but they also serve different purposes.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=156)** A user story acts as a placeholder for a conversation, a reminder that we need to get deeper into the details of something.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=164)** A use case on the other hand, can be regarded as a record of a conversation that has already happened detailing the steps of how a particular goal may or may not be achieved.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=175)** Now certain software development methodologies may favor one of these over the other, but use cases and user stories are not the direct competitors.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=184)** Nor are they necessarily a replacement for writing functional requirements.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=189)** These are all different tools in your toolbox.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=192)** Depending on the development methodology you're using, you're likely to use some combination of requirements, use cases, and user stories, but not necessarily all three.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=202)** And the order in which you create them can vary.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=205)** However you do it, describing your system in plain language is incredibly useful to identify why we're building this application and those descriptions serve as input for the next stages of the analysis and design process.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=218)** If you want to learn more about the concise user story format, check out Mike Cohn's book, User Stories Applied for [[Agile Software Development]].
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/user-stories?u=76281980&t=227)** It's a book you're likely to see recommended if you use a process like Scrum or Extreme Programming.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - user (1), - here (1), - great (1), - how (1), - perfect (1)
> **Code Keywords:** interface (2), case. (1), case, (1), this. (1), let (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Best Practices:** recommended (1)
> **Prerequisites:** you need to have (1)

#### Challenge: Jukebox use cases
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=0)** - It's time for another challenge.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=2)** This time you'll be writing use cases and user stories to help design a jukebox to go onto a spaceship so astronauts can listen to music on their way to the moon.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=12)** This is an extension of the previous challenge problem where we defined requirements for the space jukebox.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=19)** If you completed that challenge, then you can use the requirements that you defined as a basis for this challenge problem.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=25)** Otherwise, feel free to build on our partial set of requirements shown here.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=31)** Your goal for this challenge is to write at least two use cases and two user stories for the jukebox.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=37)** But, if you're feeling inspired, you can certainly write more for extra practice.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=42)** I'll leave the format and level of detail for the use cases up to you.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=46)** You can write the scenarios as a paragraph or a list of steps.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=50)** Either one is fine.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=52)** This is an open-ended challenge with a wide range of possible solutions so take some time to think it through.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=58)** And, when you're done, be sure to watch our solution video to see how we approached the problem.

#### Solution: Jukebox use cases
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=3)** - To develop our use cases and user stories for the jukebox challenge, we decided to start with the most common thing you can do with the jukebox.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=11)** - Play a song.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=12)** - The primary actor here will be the user, and I decided to format the scenario for this use case as a paragraph.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=19)** So the system identifies the user.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=22)** The user browses the library of available albums.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=25)** They select an album, then browse the songs on that album, and then finally the user selects a song and the jukebox plays it.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=34)** This may seem like an obvious sequence of events, but taking the time to actually put it down in writing now will make later parts of the design process a lot easier.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=43)** - For the next use case, let's look at a slightly more complicated scenario, selecting multiple songs to play.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=50)** I prefer to format my use cases as a list of steps.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=54)** The first four steps of this use case will have a lot in common with Olivia's use case to play a single song.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=61)** The system identifies the user, the user browses the available albums and songs, selects a song, and then the system begins playing it.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=70)** After that, though, this use case introduces some new events.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=74)** The user continues browsing and selects a second song.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=78)** The system adds that second song to a play queue, and then plays it after the first song is over.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=84)** This use case introduces the concept of a play queue that wasn't present in the first one.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=89)** - [Olivia] Now for some user stories.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=91)** If Baron has gone and filled up the play queue with his music, then as a user, I want my song to be added to the front of the long play queue so that I don't have to wait hours to hear it.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=105)** - And from a usability standpoint, as a user, I want to be identified without having to touch anything so I can use my hands to do other things.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=114)** Coming up with user stories is usually a lot quicker than writing use cases, so it's easy to keep pumping them out.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=121)** As a user, I want to sort and browse songs by artist, so that I can listen to every song by ABBA.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=128)** - That will drive the other astronauts crazy, so as the spaceship's commander, I want the ability to cancel other users' selections so I don't have to listen to disco all the way to the moon.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=140)** (snapping) Now, the prior set of requirements we came up with for the space jukebox didn't mention anything about a commander or administrator, but based on that last user story, I think that's something we'll need to include.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=155)** And that's one of the reasons why just the process of writing use cases and user stories is valuable.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-use-cases?u=76281980&t=162)** It can help uncover new requirements.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - to (1), - play (1), - the (1), - for (1), - [olivia] (1)
> **Code Keywords:** case, (1), let (1), include. (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Env Vars:** abba (1)
> **Cross-References:** coming up (1)


### 4. Domain Modeling

#### Identifying the objects
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=0)** - After defining requirements and writing some use cases or user stories we start to transition from analysis, understanding the problem we're trying to solve, to design, how we're going to organize our solution.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=13)** And I think a good context for explaining that is the design of a video game.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=18)** Olivia and I are game developers and I have a brand new idea for a game.

> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=23)** You're a spaceship and you shoot missiles at asteroids that are trying to crash into you.

>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=43)** - Good, 'cuz I've already done the analysis and written up the use cases.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=48)** - Well, with the analysis done, our next step is to create a conceptual model.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=53)** That may sound complex, but a conceptual model simply identifies the most important objects in the application and the relationship between them.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=62)** Now, I'm using the word, object, loosely here.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=65)** We're not worried about software objects quite yet, but more generically, what are the things in the application that we need to be aware of.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=73)** Not everything we pick here will become a class, but a lot of them will.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=77)** - [Olivia] To identify objects, we'll go through all of our use cases and user stories and any other written requirements to pick out all of the nouns.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=85)** - That's one benefit of actually writing out all those descriptions.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=89)** - I'll start with this use case that describes the player's asteroid dodging a missile from one of the spaceships by simply highlighting and underlying all of the nouns.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=99)** The system spawns enemy spaceship in play area.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=103)** Spaceship flies towards player asteroid and fires missile at it.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=108)** Player steers asteroid in direction to avoid missile path.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=113)** Missile flies past player asteroid and disappears offscreen.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=117)** As I'm gathering a list of potential nouns, I fight the urge to analyze or judge the words.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=123)** I don't worry if there's a better word to use or if, perhaps, we used a different noun to describe it elsewhere.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=129)** - And if you miss one, don't worry!
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=131)** It happens.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=132)** Now, we can take a first pass through our list and find if there are any obvious duplicates.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=138)** - [Olivia] Well in this use case, the word, it, refers to the asteroid.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=142)** So that's an easy one to remove.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=144)** - [Programmer] You'll often find yourself combining some of these or even splitting them up.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=149)** Picking out the nouns is merely a starting point and you'll find that sometimes attributes start to announce themselves.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=155)** - [Olivia] The word, offscreen, is really part of the concept of area.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=159)** So, offscreen might be an attribute of an area class depending on how things get organized, but we're not focused on showing that kind of data now.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=168)** So I'll remove it.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=169)** Now, to me, the noun, direction, feels questionable here.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=173)** Direction is where something goes, but should it really be an object?
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=177)** - At this point, if you're in doubt, keep it.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=180)** We could implement the asteroid's direction as an attribute with simple values to represent its velocity in each axis, and in that case we wouldn't need a separate class for direction.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=191)** On the other hand, if that direction of travel might need to be translated between different coordinate systems, then encapsulating that behavior in a direction class could be useful.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=202)** We aren't that far into the design process, so we don't know yet.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=206)** Let's keep it, because we can always make changes later.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=209)** One last item to remove.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=211)** Even though, system, was used as a noun, let's remove it here and we'll explain the reason why a little later on.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=218)** So, that leaves us with a handful of nouns from this one casual use case.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-the-objects?u=76281980&t=223)** These are just the names of potential objects and by simply drawing a box around each of those objects we have the beginning of a conceptual model.

#### Identifying class relationships
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - Once we have the potential objects picked out for our conceptional model, it's useful to indicate the main relationships or associations between those concepts by drawing lines between the boxes.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=12)** And since I drew the last diagram, it's your turn to take the marker.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=16)** - Fine.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=18)** - Many of these relationships will be obvious but we can always reference our written use cases and user stories if needed.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=25)** I know that the idea or concept of a player has some kind of relationship or interaction with the astroid.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=32)** So draw a line between those boxes.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=38)** The astroid exists within the area.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=40)** So that's another relationship.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=43)** And the spaceship and missile also exist within the area.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=49)** There's a relationship between the spaceship and the missile.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=55)** As well as the missile and the path it follows.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=60)** And though I'm not completely convinced this should be an object, the astroid and spaceship both travel in a direction.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=69)** Now, optionally, it may be useful to add a short note to actually describe the relationship.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=74)** It could be something as simple as the word uses, as in the spaceship uses the missile.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=80)** Or even better, spaceship fires missile.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=84)** Because that's more specific.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=86)** So rather than saying the player uses the astroid, we could say the player steers the astroid.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=94)** The area also contains spaceships, and the missile follows a path.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=101)** Finally, we can add some optional symbols to describe a bit more about those relationships.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=106)** For example, the game area can contain multiple spaceships, and we can describe that using the UML notation for multiplicity, representing one or more of something.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=117)** This additional notation isn't necessary but it can be useful.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=122)** The key question should be is it interesting or important enough to need to be put on the diagram.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=128)** Now it may seem like there are a lot of interesting details missing from here, and there's no way we could start writing code based on this diagram but that's okay.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=138)** This is just a conceptional model to use for communication and to prompt ideas.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=143)** The benefit of detailing these relationships is that it makes it easier to realize which objects interact with each other, meaning which objects have behaviors that affect other objects.

#### Identifying class responsibilities
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=0)** - We need to figure out the responsibilities for our conceptual objects to really identify what are, and what are not classes that we'll need to create, and just as we started by picking out nouns in our written description to create a list of potential objects we can go back to the use cases and user stories to look for verbs, and verb phrases to identify responsibilities.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=23)** - Looking at our use case for dodging a missile, we have phrases related to the space ship like spawns enemy spaceship, flies towards player, and fires missile, the player steers asteroid to avoid missile, and the missile flies past player and disappears offscreen.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=44)** Now not all of these will necessarily become behaviors, some might need to be combined, some might need to be split apart, and some may not be needed at all or will be replaced by something else.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=55)** But they're a good starting point and will often prompt other discussions.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=59)** - Something that isn't always obvious, is where these responsibilities belong.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=64)** Particularly if they affect different objects.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=67)** Since our use case here has an active perspective, it's oriented around what initiates a behavior not necessarily whose responsibility it is to perform that behavior.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=79)** When asking the question of where should a behavior live, or whose responsibility is it?
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=85)** Remember that an object should be responsible for itself.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=89)** - So for example, the idea of steering the asteroid, it's pretty obvious what that means and that it's going to be initiated by the player.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=98)** But, even though it's the player that wants to steer the asteroid, I shouldn't write code in the player class to directly change the inner state of an asteroid object.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=108)** The player should really ask that of the asteroid object.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=112)** The player should tell the asteroid where it wants to go but the asteroid is responsible for actually changing its state to move there.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=118)** - Figuring out whose job it is to do all of these things can be tricky, so let's take a pass through the list of verb phrases from our use case, and make an initial attempt at distributing them amongst the conceptual objects.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=132)** Starting with the phrase, spawn enemy spaceship.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=135)** Although the game system will initiate that happening the spaceship object should be responsible for creating itself and establishing its location within the play area, the next phrase, flies towards player is an action for that spaceship and I'm going to rephrase that to simply move.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=155)** Next on the list, a spaceship initiates firing a missile but a missile object should be responsible for spawning itself, knowing where it's going and what it's doing, and when it comes to the player steering the asteroid, the asteroid should handle moving itself.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=172)** Now the next one is a bit tricky, avoid missile path, what that really means is we need to detect if and when a missile collides with the asteroid so we could give that responsibility of detecting a collision with the asteroid to the missile.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=188)** But we could also put the responsibility of detecting collisions onto the asteroid.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=193)** Either way could work and at this point it may not be clear which is better or if it even matters.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=199)** For now I'll leave the responsibility to detect collisions with the asteroid because I'll need to know when it collides with other things like a spaceship for other use cases.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=209)** Now, for the last two items on the list, flies past player can be simplified to moving the missile and for the missile to disappear when it goes offscreen it will need to detect when it goes out of bounds.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=222)** There are always multiple successful ways to implement ideas in object oriented programming, and even this simple model could have been arranged in several different ways.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=232)** - You gave a couple responsibilities to the asteroid, the spaceship and the missile, but the player didn't get any shouldn't the player get some responsibilities?
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=240)** 'Cause you know, they're playing the game?
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=243)** - Not necessarily, keep in mind that this isn't showing who initiates these actions but where the responsibility lies in performing them.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=253)** The player is still going to cause things to happen by requesting behaviors of other objects.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=258)** It's a common mistake for new developers to give way too much behavior to a single actor such as the player because the player's what drives the encounter.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=267)** In object oriented design, an object that knows too much or does too much is often called a god object.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=274)** - Along those lines, an issue that often comes up is creating a system object and putting a huge amount of responsibility in it.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=282)** It happens when people see phrases like system spawns spaceship and system detects missile collision.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=290)** Recognize the phrases like system reduces player life counter really means that some part of the system reduces the life counter.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=298)** Some part of the system spawns a new player asteroid.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=302)** So while there's often a built-in system or application object included in many object oriented programming languages.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=310)** If your own design contains a central system or master object, that's filled with lots of unrelated behaviors, and just seems to exist to control everything else around it?
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=322)** That's a clue that you're still thinking like a procedural programmer.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=325)** Responsibilities should be distributed between your objects not stored in one master object an object should be responsible for itself, as much as possible.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-class-responsibilities?u=76281980&t=335)** That'll make maintaining and updating the application much easier in the future.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - we (1), - looking (1), - something (1), - so (1), - figuring (1)
> **Code Keywords:** else. (1), let (1), pass (1), case, (1)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1), is a  (1), means that (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1), common mistake (1)
> **Cross-References:** go back to (1)

#### CRC cards
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Man] One technique that can be useful during this stage of Object Oriented Design are CRC cards.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=7)** Which stands for: Class, Responsibility, Collaboration.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=11)** CRC cards contain the same information as the Conceptual Object Diagram, just in a different format.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=17)** They're drawn on index cards, and they're meant to be simple.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=20)** Easy to create, hand around, discuss, spread out on a conference table, and you dispose of it if you make a mistake, or change your mind.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=29)** Each CRC cards represents one class, and it has three sections.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=34)** The first C is the name of the class at the top, which is usually underlined.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=39)** The R is the Responsibilities of the class, the things that it needs to take care of.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=45)** And the second C is for the Collaborators, the other classes it interacts with.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=50)** CRC cards typically use this format with the responsibilities taking up the left two-thirds of the card, and the collaborators on what's remaining to the right.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=60)** You may also hear these referred to as CRH cards for: Component, Responsibilities, and Helper.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=67)** Those are effectively the same thing, just using different terms.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=71)** Start creating these cards by using the nouns, and your use case and user story descriptions, to help identify potential classes, and the verbs, and verb phrases, to help identify responsibilities.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=83)** - [Woman] So a card to represent our Missile class could have things like: Fly through space, Destroy asteroid, and Disappear offscreen, as responsibilities.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=92)** I'm not worried about the official method names here, I'm just using whatever phrases makes sense to say what this class needs to do.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=99)** We can refine these later.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=101)** Some will be combined and some will split apart.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=103)** If it's obvious which of the other classes will be Collaborators.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=107)** Meaning what other classes will interact with this one, then I can write those too.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=111)** I know this Missile class will interact with the spaceship that fires it, the play area it flies through, and the asteroid it blows up.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=118)** So I'll add those to the right hand side.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=120)** - [Man] One of the great things about CRC cards is that when you physically start to do this, and start creating a pile of cards, most people naturally find themselves moving related cards together.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=131)** And that helps a lot in figuring out the natural collaborators, and the way these objects interact.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=136)** And that's one reason you shouldn't jump to using an electronic tool for doing this, because there's a lot of value in the physicality of moving things around.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=145)** - [Woman] Using physical cards also enforces a constraint.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=148)** You can keep adding more and more index cards to your collection, but, if you need more than one CRC card for a single class, that's a clue that you may need to redesign that class, because, you're trying to give it too much responsibility.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=162)** Whether you choose to use CRC cards, or a Conceptual Diagram, or some other method of your own.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/crc-cards?u=76281980&t=169)** You should finish this phase of the object oriented design process, with at least the names and core responsibilities for the first set of classes you intend to code.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** class, (5), for: (2), this, (2)
> **Env Vars:** crc (7), crh (1)
> **Speakers:** - [man] (2), - [woman] (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1), find (1)
> **Definitions:** stands for (1)
