#### Solution: Jukebox conceptual model
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=0)** (high chiming tones)
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=3)** - The first step towards building our conceptual model for the Space Jukebox Challenge is to identify the objects.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=10)** By finding the nouns and the use cases and user stories.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=13)** So, in this particular use case, for playing multiple songs, we have nouns like system, user, library, album, list, song, and queue.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=25)** Now, I'm not putting too much thought into what each of those really mean yet, I'm just practicing my grammar skills by picking out the nouns.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=32)** I'll do the same process with one of our user stories which contains nouns like commander, ability, user, selection, disco, and the moon.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=43)** We should go through the same process with the rest of our use cases and user stories, but for simplicity, we'll stick with just those two for now.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=51)** That gives us a list of possible objects that we can begin cleaning up.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=56)** We want to avoid having a master system object in our conceptual model, so I'll remove that from the list.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=63)** The words disco and moon were colorful language from the user story but aren't really relevant to the functionality of the jukebox, so I'll cut those too.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=73)** I don't think the word ability belongs here as an object because what it's referring to is a behavior which we'll capture separately.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=81)** And I think the concept of a user's selection is captured by the song they select.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=87)** The word list in this context is really just an album which is a list of songs.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=92)** So that duplicate can go as well.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=95)** And for one last minor adjustment, I'll change the term commander to the more common administrator, or admin, role because that's what it really means in this scenario.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=105)** That leaves us with our initial set of six objects.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=109)** The user, library, album, song, queue, and admin.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=115)** - While Barron was busy looking for objects, I turned my attention towards the responsibilities.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=121)** I looked at the same use case as Barron, except instead of picking out nouns, I picked out verb phrases, identifies user, browses library, selects an album, and so on.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=132)** There were two of these phrases that I decided I did not need to keep.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=136)** Continues browsing is basically just repeating a behavior that was captured in the previous verb phrases, browses library and browses list of songs.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=146)** And the same goes for the phrase selects a second song.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=150)** That's just another instance of user selects a song, so I'll go ahead and cut that out.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=156)** Jumping over to the user story, I found two verb phrases, cancel other user selections and listen to disco.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=165)** I definitely want the jukebox to include the ability to cancel bad song choices.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=169)** But listening to disco is a more specific variation of the previous responsibility to play a selected song, so it can go away.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=178)** That leaves us with a list of nine verb phrases which I've reworded slightly to better stand out of context.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=186)** Now, we have the fun task of determining the responsibilities that each of these verb phrases actually represent and where they belong.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=194)** - I've created index cards for each of our six objects and taped them to our whiteboard.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=198)** - Great, so the first verb phrase on our list is browse library of albums.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=205)** - Well, the user will initiate that action with the library so draw a line between those two objects and label that relationship as the user browses the music library.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=217)** There's also a relationship between the library and album objects because a library contains multiple albums.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=225)** If we wanted to make these into formal CRC cards, we would write the collaborators on the right side of the card, but since we've drawn those lines to represent the same thing, we'll leave those off the cards.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=236)** This is sort of a hybrid approach.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=238)** So those are the relationships for the music library, but for the user to browse it, the library's going to need to display the album.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=247)** That's one responsibility down, only eight more to go.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=251)** - I've gone ahead and added the rest of the responsibilities and relationships to our conceptual model.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=256)** - Thanks.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=256)** - [Woman] The phrase, browse songs and album, turned into a responsibility for an album object to display songs.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=263)** The album also got the responsibility to select a song, and above that, the library got the responsibility to select an album.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=272)** The song object itself is responsible for actually playing the song, and the rest of the responsibilities fell under the queue.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=279)** The phrase add song to queue became simply add song.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=284)** When it comes to playing the next song in the queue, the queue is only responsible for getting the next song because the song object itself has the play responsibility.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=294)** Canceling a song in the queue turned into the queue's responsibility to remove a song.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=299)** And finally we give the responsibility to identify the user to the queue as well.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=304)** - [Barron] Now, it was a bit tricky figuring out where to place the responsibility of identifying the user.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=310)** And I'll admit, I was tempted to create a new system object for it.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=314)** - [Woman] But we took a step back and asked, which object or objects care about knowing the users identity, and our answer was.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=322)** - [Both] The queue.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=323)** - The queue needs to know which user added each song to the playlist so it can reorder songs appropriately.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=330)** - Depending on how the jukebox actually ends up implementing the process of determining the user's ID, we may need to add more objects to this conceptual model.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-conceptual-model?u=76281980&t=338)** But for now, keeping the responsibility to identify the user here reminds us that it's something the queue needs to know.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - the (2), - i (2), - [woman] (2), - while (1), - great (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Code Keywords:** case, (1)
> **Env Vars:** crc (1)


### 5. Class Diagrams

#### Creating class diagrams: Attributes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=0)** - All of our previous work, writing requirements and use cases and building a conceptual model, was to figure out our first set of classes, so now it's time to create a proper class diagram to visually represent them.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=14)** And this is where specific object-oriented principles, like inheritance and polymorphism, can start coming into play.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=22)** The most common format to use is the UML class diagram.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=26)** These can get pretty advanced, but for this course, we'll stick with using just the most common aspects.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=32)** Each class should have a name written in the singular form, not plural, and the standard is to use an uppercase first letter, so things like Asteroid, Missile, Spaceship.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=44)** Now, you won't know all of the attributes yet, because the focus so far has been on class responsibilities and behaviors, but start by writing down any obvious ones, and you can always add more later.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=57)** Use whatever naming convention is typical for your programming language.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=61)** I'm using the common camel case format here, which starts with a lowercase first letter and then uses uppercase for additional words.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=70)** While it's common to just write the attribute names, you can also suggest a data type using a colon.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=76)** So colon String for callSign, I want each of the enemy spaceships to have a unique name, colon Boolean for the shieldActive flag, colon Integer, and colon Coordinate.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=90)** I can also describe a default value, using an equal sign after the data type.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=95)** Not all attributes need that, just where it's relevant and important.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=99)** As you're initially creating class diagrams, don't get hung up on knowing the exact data types to use.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=105)** Should shieldStrength be a 16-bit integer or a 32-bit integer, or even 64?
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=111)** We'll get to those details later, during implementation.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=115)** But for now, the diagram makes it clear that we need some sort of integer.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=119)** Along the same lines, coordinates for the position attribute could be implemented several different ways.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=125)** It could be split into two separate attributes for the x-coordinate and the y-coordinate.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=130)** Or it could use a more complex data type, like a tuple or an array to store a pair of values.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=136)** Or I might create my own custom class for coordinates.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-attributes?u=76281980&t=140)** Again, details that we'll need to work out later.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Identifiers:** callsign (1), shieldactive (1), shieldstrength (1)
> **Code Keywords:** type. (1), type, (1)
> **Env Vars:** uml (1)
> **Speakers:** - all (1)

#### Creating class diagrams: Behaviors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=0)** - When it comes to adding behaviors to our class diagram, we should have a good idea of what to write here from creating our conceptional object model and CRC cards.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=10)** I'll use the same camel case format as the attribute names.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=14)** But since these represent operations, they're usually named as short verb phrases; getShieldStrength, reduceShield, and simply move.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=23)** It's common practice to name methods that modify and retrieve attributes as get instant operations rather than things like change or retrieve.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=34)** And some languages will even automatically generate getter and setter methods for you.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=39)** Since these will eventually turn into code, we'll add parenthesis containing any parameters.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=45)** For example, the reduceShield method takes an integer as it's input parameter.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=51)** On the other side, I could also add a return type by putting a colon after the parenthesis followed by the return type I'm expecting back from the operation.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=61)** - Now these classes may have a lot of functionality internally, but the focus here is on public visibility.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=68)** What are the operations that other objects need to know about?
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=72)** And this is in line with the principle of encapsulation.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=75)** To hide as much of the implementation as possible, and only share what's absolutely necessary to expose to other classes.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=82)** You'll commonly see plus and minus signs before the attributes and methods in UML class diagrams.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=89)** Which is referred to as controlling visibility.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=93)** Minus indicates that a member should be private to the class, meaning it's not directly accessible by other objects, and plus means the member should be public.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=103)** So, we've made the shieldStrength attribute private here but we'll allow other objects to retrieve it using the public getShieldStrength method which returns in integer value.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=114)** We want the setPosition method to only be used internally by other methods in this object, such as the move method, so we decided to keep it private.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=124)** The rule here is to leave as many attributes and methods private as possible, and only make something public if you know another object will need to use it.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=133)** - You know, these class diagram aren't that hard to create.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=136)** And it seems like we could've saved a lot of time if we just jumped straight to trying out classes.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=142)** - We could do that but what often happens when people jump straight to object creation, is they focus on the data.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=149)** They decide they need a space ship object but the way they start to build the spaceship is to have a call sign, size, shape, color, mass, thrust, position, velocity, shield strength, the obvious attributes.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=161)** - So what are the behaviors?
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=163)** - That's the problem.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=164)** It might be true that these objects need those attributes, but it's the wrong initial focus.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=169)** If you find yourself defining classes that are strangely devoid of any behavior, you might want to revisit those responsibilities.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=177)** Do a little work with the requirements, written descriptions, and the conceptional model, or CRC cards.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/creating-class-diagrams-behaviors?u=76281980&t=182)** Your focus should really be on what object do rather than just viewing them as data structures.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** public (4), private (4), class, (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** getshieldstrength (2), reduceshield (2), shieldstrength (1), setposition (1)
> **Speakers:** - when (1), - now (1), - you (1), - we (1), - so (1)
> **Env Vars:** crc (2), uml (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1), find (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), such as (1)

#### Converting class diagrams into code
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=1)** - Olivia, so far we've looked at all the ideas and concepts of object orientation, but at the end of the day all of this design work we're doing will need to be converted into a programming language.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=13)** So, can we start looking at code yet?
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=15)** - Yes, Barron.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=16)** It's code time.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=18)** - Finally!
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=18)** There are lots of different object oriented languages to choose from and each has their own unique way of doing things.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=25)** So we want to show you that the high level concepts we've been discussing here work across languages, even though the implementation details, like syntax, will be different.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=35)** To demonstrate that, I've simplified the spaceship class to only have two attributes, a public call sign, which is a string and a private integer to represent its shield strength.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=46)** And it has two methods that are both public, fire missile, which returns a string and reduce shield, which returns nothing or at least that's what this class diagram says, but it does take an integer as input.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=61)** So, to turn this into Java code, we would use the words, public class spaceship, to actually create the class with opening and closing curly braces to wrap its contents.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=74)** The first section containing instance variables is labeled with two forward slashes that represent the comment and these are variables that belong to an instance of this class.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=85)** Meaning that, every spaceship object we instantiate from this class will have its own copy of these variables.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=91)** We've used the Java keywords, public and private, to set the visibility levels of those variables to match the UML diagram.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=99)** - If you're unfamiliar with Java or other curly braced formatted languages, don't worry about the syntax here.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=106)** We're showing you this to get a sense of the kind of key words languages could use, how variables are named, and methods and so on.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=114)** And to give you a sense of how similar many of these languages can be.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=118)** In fact, if we take that spaceship class at the same level of complexity and convert it into C#, it would look identical to Java.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=127)** Java and C# are very similar and they implement a lot of these ideas the same way.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=133)** We have the two instance variables and two methods.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=137)** One called fire missile, that returns a string and one called reduce shield that takes an integer called, amount, and returns void, meaning that it returns nothing.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=147)** Swift is another curly braced formatted language, but it has some differences from Java and C#.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=154)** The syntax for declaring instance variables uses the keyword, var, and it specifies a type at the end of the statement.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=161)** We've also followed the naming convention of adding an underscore to the front of the shield strength variable to indicate that it's private.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=169)** The symbols and syntax differ, but it has the same basic elements as its C# counterpart.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=175)** - If we make the jump to my favorite language, Python, we drop the curly braces because Python uses line spacing to indicate separate sections of code.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=185)** Another noticeable difference with Python is that our two instance variables are declared inside of a special method called, init, which gets called when we create an instance of this class.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=197)** Variables declared outside of that method will have a different scope, which we'll get to a bit later.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=204)** Finally, Python doesn't really differentiate between public and private variables.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=209)** It puts trust in the hands of the programmer not to do anything foolish.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=213)** So we've added an underscore to the shield strength variable name to indicate that it should be treated as if it were private.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=220)** And for one last example, Ruby, which also leaves out the curly braces and has a simple syntax because, like Python, Ruby is a dynamic language.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=231)** Meaning that it does not require as much information regarding variable types or return types.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=236)** The at symbol in front of the variable names, call sign and shield strength, indicate that they're instance variables and Ruby's naming convention for variables uses an underscore between words rather than an uppercase letter.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=251)** Now, there are many more languages than we covered here and some have substantially different implementations of these ideas, such as JavaScript or Lua or Go.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/converting-class-diagrams-into-code?u=76281980&t=261)** Those don't even have official classes, but the generic object oriented concepts that we're exploring here still apply.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** public (5), private (5), class. (2), var (1), finally, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** python (5), ruby (3), make (1)
> **Speakers:** - if (2), - olivia (1), - yes (1), - finally (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)

#### Instantiating classes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=1)** - When we write the class for a spaceship in our video game, we're creating the blueprint to build the spaceship.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=7)** Now, the blueprint itself isn't a usable object, but from that class, we can instantiate or create one or more instances of that type of spaceship object.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=17)** And to do that, many object-oriented languages use the keyword new.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=23)** In Java, we'd say Spaceship then the variable name myShip equals new Spaceship with open and close parenthesis.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=33)** And that instantiation line would be identical in C#.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=37)** C++ looks very similar to Java and C# except it's using the asterisk to indicate a pointer.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=44)** Don't get caught up in syntax here, these are just some examples to show how languages differ.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=50)** Ruby is unique in that it puts the keyword new after the class name.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=55)** But not all languages use new.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=58)** In Python, it's simply myShip equals Spaceship, and Swift uses the keyword let to assign the newly instantiated Spaceship to the variable named myShip.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=71)** When we use one of these instantiation statements, shown here in a typical Java or C# format, in the background, the computer will allocate a section of memory to hold the new object, including space for each of the variables in it.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=85)** It initializes those variables to some value, returns a reference to that object in memory, and that gets assigned to a variable named myShip so we can begin using it.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=96)** Now, you should always consider what the internal state of an object will be immediately after you instantiate it.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=103)** Our simplified Spaceship class defines two attributes, callSign as a string and shieldStrength as an integer, and when the object is created, they'll be given some default values.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=115)** In Java, the default value for a string is Null and the default value for an integer is zero.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=121)** If we don't take those default values into consideration and our program tries to use them, that could lead to some undesired behaviors, and that's dangerous.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=131)** Now, we could go and set those values immediately after we create the object but it would be better if we created the object in a meaningful state to begin with.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=141)** To do that, we'll use what's called the constructor.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=143)** It's a special method that gets called when the object is instantiated to help configure it.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=149)** We can use the constructor method to make sure that any variables belonging to the object are set to the values we want when the object is created.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=158)** In Java or C#, you create a constructor by simply defining a new method in the class with the same name as the class itself.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=166)** So, the constructor for the Spaceship class will be a method named Spaceship.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=172)** However, unlike other methods, the constructor does not have a return type because you never call it yourself.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=179)** It gets called when you use the keyword new to instantiate a new object.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=185)** Inside the constructor, we set the initial state that we want these instance variables to have.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=190)** We'll set the callSign to be The nameless ship and give it a shieldStrength of 100.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=196)** Now, when we use that same line of code to instantiate the object, it will be initialized with those specific values that we want instead of the default values of Null and zero.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/instantiating-classes?u=76281980&t=207)** In a UML class diagram, this type of basic constructor method that doesn't take any arguments is represented as a method with the same name as the class.

> **Env Vars:** uml (1)

> **Best Practices:** you should always (1)

#### Class with multiple constructors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've defined a constructor to instantiate our spaceship object, and it sets the callsign attribute to a value of the nameless ship.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=9)** But, what if I don't want all of the ships we instantiate to have a callsign of the nameless ship?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=14)** That'd get confusing.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=16)** - Well, most languages will let us create multiple constructor methods through a process called overloading, which allows a class to have more than one method with the same name, but different sets of input parameters.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=30)** To do that in Java, we'll create a second method in this spaceship class, also called spaceship, but this one takes a parameter.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=38)** Just a string, which I've called name and that gets assigned to the callsign variable.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=43)** Now when we instantiate a spaceship object, we have two ways of doing it.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=48)** We can use the word new with no parameters, like before, which will call the first constructor method to give us the nameless ship, or, we can use the word new, along with a string parameter.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=61)** When we include the string parameter, it's going to call the overloaded constructor, which sets those attributes to different values.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=70)** Overloading multiple constructors gives us flexibility to pass in information when actually creating the object.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=77)** And in UML, that would be represented with something like this.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=80)** We have two methods, with the same name as the class, one with a parameter and one without.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=87)** This is a very simple example, but constructors become crucial when you have more complex objects.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=93)** It's often very important to make sure objects aren't instantiated in an invalid state, particularly when one object needs to contain other objects and often has to create them as part of that instantiation process.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=107)** - Now constructors give birth to an object, but on the other end of an object's lifetime, is the idea of a destructor.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=114)** The destructor is a method that gets called when an object is no longer needed and is being disposed of.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=121)** Languages that use garbage collection use something called a finalizer, rather than a destructor, but the concept is the same.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=129)** It's a place to put some code that will automatically be called when the object is destroyed.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=134)** Destructors are typically used if you have an object that's holding a resource.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/class-with-multiple-constructors?u=76281980&t=138)** Say it has a document open on the file system or it's connected to a database, and you need to make sure that the object releases its connection to that resource, before it's destroyed.
