>
> **Code Keywords:** class, (2), let (1), new, (1), pass (1), this. (1)
> **Speakers:** - we (1), - well (1), - now (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Env Vars:** uml (1)

#### Static attributes and methods
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=1)** - When we instantiate a bunch of enemy spaceships for our game, we'll want each of those spaceship objects to keep track of its own remaining shield strength independent of the others.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=11)** That way, if the player's asteroid smashes into one of the ships, it'll reduce its shield strength, while the other ships remain unaffected.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=20)** We've accomplished that by storing the ship's shield strength as an instance variable, which means each instance of the spaceship class will have its own separate copy.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=31)** - What do you think about adding a different difficulty setting to our game?
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=35)** We could scale the toughness of all the ships, making their shields easier or harder to destroy.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=41)** - Sounds good to me.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=42)** - But unlike shield strength, the spaceships' toughness value should not vary from ship to ship.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=49)** If the player decides to increase or decrease the game's difficulty, then all of the ships should increase or decrease their toughness the same amount.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=58)** - Well, if we create toughness as another instance variable, that would require us to individually update each of the spaceships any time there's a change to keep them all synchronized.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=70)** We could do it that way, but it's not very efficient, especially as the number of spaceships grows.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=76)** The smarter route would be to create a static variable, which is a variable that's shared across all objects in the same class.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=84)** This is also referred to as a class level or shared variable.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=88)** Those terms mean the same thing.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=90)** Now, with a static variable, there's only one copy of toughness to update.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=95)** If one spaceship changes that shared toughness value, it changes for all of them.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=100)** - Isn't that just a global variable?
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=102)** I've always been told to avoid using global variables.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=105)** - Sort of, it's global amongst the spaceships but not the rest of the program.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=110)** We didn't just toss it into some global dumping ground for variables.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=114)** That's not good programming.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=116)** But we do want all of the spaceships to have the same toughness, so it's shared within the class, but still encapsulated.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=123)** - Static seems like a bad name for it, because to me, static means unchanging.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=129)** - Well, it is unchanging in the number of copies of a static variable never changes.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=134)** There will only be one.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=136)** As for the number of instance level variables, that can vary from run to run, depending on how many objects the program creates that time around.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=144)** As a basic example of how this is handled in code, if we're using a language like C Sharp or Java, we would include the word static in the line that declares the variable.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=155)** Of course, other languages handle it differently.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=158)** Python doesn't use the static keyword.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=161)** Instead, variables that are declared within a class, but outside of a method, are considered static or class variables, and in Ruby, class variables are declared using two @ symbols instead of just one @ symbol for instance variables.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=176)** When it comes to accessing instance level variables, we use the name of the object to get to them.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=182)** So to access the call sign of the spaceship object named Ship One, we would use ship1.callSign.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=191)** But if we want to access a static variable, we need to use the class name, not a particular instance name.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=199)** So spaceship.toughness.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=202)** Even if you haven't instantiated any objects of this class yet, you can still use any variables defined as static.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=209)** So we could set the toughness for the entire spaceship class before instantiating any spaceship objects.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=215)** - Now, in addition to static variables, we can also create static methods that exist at the class level.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=222)** Like when creating static variables, in Java or C Sharp we simply add the keyword static to a method declaration to indicate that it's a static method, and there's only one of it.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=233)** Since static methods exist at the class level, they can only access static variables.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=239)** They can't touch instance variables, and static methods are always called using the class name, not the name of an instance.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/static-attributes-and-methods?u=76281980&t=246)** In UML diagrams, static members of a class are typically denoted with an underline, so here toughness is a static attribute, and increase difficulty is a static method or behavior.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** static (21), class, (2), require (1), class. (1)
> **Speakers:** - well (2), - when (1), - what (1), - sounds (1), - but (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), defined as (1)
> **CLI Commands:** python (1), ruby (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** callsign (1)
> **Env Vars:** uml (1)
> **Analogies:** for instance (1)

#### Challenge: Jukebox class diagrams
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=3)** - It's challenge time again.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=5)** This challenge builds on the previous one, which involved creating the conceptual model of a spaceship jukebox for astronauts to listen to music.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=13)** For this challenge, I want you to create a UML class diagram, based on that conceptual model of a jukebox.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=20)** If you've been following along and creating your own solutions for the challenges thus far, then I encourage you to continue using your own conceptual model or CRC cards for this.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=31)** Otherwise, feel free to use the conceptual model we created in the previous challenge and solution videos, which is shown here.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=39)** It contains six objects, the user, library, album, song, cue, and admin.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=46)** The corresponding responsibilities are listed under the object's name and the interesting relationships and collaborations between objects, are represented with lines.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=55)** To create the UML class diagrams, you'll need to think about the attributes and methods each class will need to accomplish its responsibilities.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=64)** You'll likely need to make some assumptions along the way, and that's great practice.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=68)** We've intentionally kept these challenges open-ended.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=71)** There are lots of possible solutions here, so take some time to think it through, and when you're done, watch our solution video to see how we approached this problem.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** uml (2), crc (1)
> **Code Keywords:** continue (1), this. (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Prerequisites:** you'll need (1)
> **Speakers:** - it (1)

#### Solution: Jukebox class diagrams
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=3)** - Let's take a look at our solution and how we converted our conceptual model into a class diagram for a spaceship jukebox.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=11)** There wasn't an obvious place to start here so we arbitrarily decided to begin with the song class because, well, songs are pretty essential to a jukebox.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=21)** In regards to attributes, at a minimum, each song should have a title, so the user knows which song they're choosing and it should have an artist because one of our requirements was being able to sort songs by artist.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=35)** Both of those attributes could be represented as strings and we decided to keep them private to the class.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=41)** That means we should include a public get method for other objects to retrieve the song's title and artist.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=48)** Finally, the song isn't very useful if it can't be played.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=51)** So, we included a play method.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=53)** Now, how that song actually gets played, is it on a record, a cd, an mp3 stored in a database?
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=61)** It doesn't matter here, they will all get handled under the hood in the implementation of the song class.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=67)** Other objects only need to know that all song objects have a play method they can call.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=73)** - The next class we focused on was the album, which is a collection of songs.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=77)** And, according to our conceptual model, an album is responsible for displaying the songs in it and selecting a song.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=83)** To do that, we gave the album class two attributes, a list of strings representing the titles of the songs and a list containing the song objects themselves that belong on that album.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=95)** To browse a song on an album, a user can call the getTitles method, which returns a list of strings with the song titles.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=104)** And, once the user has chosen a song, they can pass the title to the getSong method to retrieve the actual song object.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=111)** Now, there are lots of other ways you could handle the responsibilities of displaying and selecting songs.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=117)** We just chose to go that route.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=119)** - We decided to store titles in a list of strings.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=123)** But, would it make more sense to store them in an array, instead?
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=127)** - Whether we stored those in a list or an array or some other type of structure, it's really an implementation detail that will be influenced by the programming language we use.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=137)** - For the UML diagram, all we really want to show is that we're storing a collection of one or more strings and a collection of one or more songs.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=146)** So, using the multiplicity element here is a better way to represent that.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=150)** Moving on, the library is a collection of albums in the same way that an album is a collection of songs.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=156)** With similar responsibilities to display and select.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=160)** So, to create the library class diagram, we basically copied the class diagram for an album and replaced the word song with the word album.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=169)** Again, this is a simple implementation and it'll accomplish the library's responsibilities.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=174)** - Now, turning our attention to the queue class, a queue is effectively a collection of songs much like an album.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=182)** But, the queue has a very different set of responsibilities so we can't simply copy the album class like we did for the library.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=190)** The queue's first responsibility of adding songs to the queue translates into a method simply called addSong, which takes a song object as input and it adds that song to an internal list of songs to play.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=204)** Since the song class holds the responsibility of actually playing a song, we handled the queue's second responsibility, to play the next song, by giving it a getNextSong method that returns the next song object in the queue.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=218)** And from there, you can call that song's own play method.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=222)** The third responsibility, to let the administrator cancel songs in the queue, simply became a removeSong method.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=229)** - Now the queue's final responsibility, to identify the user that's adding songs to the queue, is a bit trickier.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=235)** The first thing that it implies is that the user class has some way of being identified.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=241)** So, to keep our example simple, we created the user class with a private string attribute representing its id and a public getId method to retrieve it.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=251)** The queue will need that id string whenever a user adds a song to the queue.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=256)** So we modified the addSong function to include a second string parameter to take in the user's id.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=262)** Just because a queue class has a responsibility to identify the user, doesn't necessarily mean it needs a separate method to accomplish that.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=271)** - And for the final admin class, our conceptual model doesn't have much information so we made a few assumptions.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=278)** We assumed that an administrator would have their own unique id similar to a user.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=284)** So we gave the admin class the corresponding attribute and method.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=288)** We also assumed that the admin would have some additional capabilities over a regular user so we gave the admin a method to create new users and a method to manage the queue.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=299)** So, that's it, the first round of class diagrams we came up with.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/solution-jukebox-class-diagrams?u=76281980&t=304)** And that's just one of an infinite number of ways to approach this challenge.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), private (2), class. (2), public (2), class, (2)
> **Speakers:** - now (2), - let (1), - the (1), - we (1), - whether (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** addsong (2), gettitles (1), getsong (1), getnextsong (1), removesong (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (6)
> **CLI Commands:** cd (1), make (1)
> **Env Vars:** uml (1)
> **Analogies:** similar to (1)


### 6. Inheritance and Composition

#### Identifying inheritance situations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=0)** - Inheritance is one of the four key concepts of object orientation.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=5)** It allows one or more classes to inherit the attributes and methods of another class, referred to as the parent or superclass.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=13)** The inheriting subclasses or child classes automatically have all of the attributes and methods of their parent class.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=21)** And they can have their own unique attributes and methods in addition to those.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=26)** The advantages of code reuse and code maintenance make inheritance a really powerful tool if you can recognize when to use it.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=34)** - Hey, Baron.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=36)** I've been designing different types of space ships for a video game.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=39)** I've got a star fighter that can shoot missiles at a player. - Mm-hmm.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=42)** - And a cargo shuttle that carries, well, cargo.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=44)** If the player asteroid's smashes into the cargo shuttle, it acts like a pinata that drops power ups into space.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=50)** - Ha, cool.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=52)** Well I sense there's some sort of relationship going on here, and the best way to identify if it's inheritance, is with two simple words, is a.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=60)** Inheritance describes an is a relationship between objects.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=65)** Now that's not some technical term.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=68)** It's just plain language, how you would talk about things in the real world.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=72)** So I could say that a star fighter is a spaceship, or a cargo shuttle is a spaceship.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=80)** Both of those make sense.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=82)** But saying that a cargo shuttle is a star fighter does not.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=86)** Those two things have a different relationship with each other, not inheritance.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=90)** - I find that using the phrase is a kind of, or is a type of, is sometimes easier to use than is a.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=98)** So a star fighter is a kind of spaceship, or a cargo shuttle is a type of spaceship.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=105)** - Sure, those are just slightly different phrases that mean the same thing.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=108)** Even saying that something is kind of like something else, could be an indicator for inheritance.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=115)** These help us identify when they're might be some shared attributes between objects, and if we think about it, we can save some time and avoid reinventing the wheel.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=123)** - Where some people hit their first problem identifying inheritance, is when they start sketching out classes, picking the nouns from the written descriptions.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=132)** As they scan those objects, they can tell that there's some sort of relationship between cargo and cargo shuttle.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=139)** But is that an inheritance relationship?
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=142)** This is where the question comes in.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=143)** Could you say that cargo is a cargo shuttle?
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=147)** - [Man] Well no, and I wouldn't say that a cargo shuttle is cargo either, so there might be some relationship there, cargo shuttles might have cargo, but that's a different relationship than inheritance.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=159)** Since I could say that a cargo shuttle is a spaceship, and a star fighter is a spaceship, that's a clue that I might be able to share some behavior in that area.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=170)** If I put those two classes next to each other, it's easy to see that they have several attributes and methods in common.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=177)** The star fighter and cargo shuttle both have attributes for shield active, shield strength, and position, as well as methods called set shield and move.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=187)** So I'll strip out those common elements and put them into a super class called spaceship, that star fighter and cargo shuttle sub classes will inherit from.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=197)** That's indicated in UML diagrams with an open arrow that look like a wedge.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=202)** The star fighter's method to fire missiles and the cargo shuttle's method to drop cargo remain in their respective sub classes, because those behaviors are unique to each one.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=213)** From here, we can easily define more types of spaceships, like a warp cruiser, that can automatically inherit all of the elements of the space ship super class.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=223)** Now there may be times when some of those inherited methods don't quite work for a specific subclass.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=229)** Maybe our warp cruiser moves differently than a generic spaceship, because after all, it travels at warp speed, and many languages will allow a subclass to replace the implementation of an inherited method through a process called overriding.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=245)** So the warp cruiser class could override just the move method to use it's own unique version of it.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=252)** Inheritance can also extend multiple levels deep.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=255)** If there are going to be other types of vehicles in our game, than we might create a higher level vehicle class for spaceship to inherit from.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=262)** The is a relationship still holds true here.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=266)** A warp cruiser is a spaceship, and a spaceship is a vehicle.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=271)** - Now, a word of warning.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=273)** It's common for new object orientated developers to overemphasize inheritance.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=277)** And to come up with class diagrams with five levels of inheritance for everything.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=283)** Don't go looking for inheritance, because inheritance usually announces itself.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=287)** If you find yourself creating one or two class diagrams without any inheritance, don't worry.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/identifying-inheritance-situations?u=76281980&t=293)** It's perfectly fine.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** cargo (19), make (2), find (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (14)
> **Code Keywords:** class. (2), super (2), from. (2), class, (1), else, (1)
> **Speakers:** - inheritance (1), - hey (1), - and (1), - ha (1), - i (1)
> **Env Vars:** uml (1)
> **Analogies:** kind of like (1)
> **Warnings:** warning (1)

#### Using inheritance
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I want to show you a few examples of how inheritance is implemented across several different languages to help you recognize inheritance when you see it in code.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=9)** But don't worry about memorizing syntax here, this is just an overview.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=14)** If I wanted to find a new class called CargoShuttle that inherits from an existing Spaceship class, in Java I would use the keyword extend followed by the name of the class I wanted to inherit from.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=27)** So CargoShuttle extends Spaceship.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=30)** In c#, I would use a colon, which is a fairly common way to indicate that CargoShuttle inherits from Spaceship.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=38)** C++ and Swift both use a similar type of colon notation as well.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=42)** Now Python is a bit different.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=45)** Python puts the super class that the new subclass will inherit from in parentheses.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=50)** And Ruby has its own way of saying that CargoShuttle inherits from Spaceship using the less than symbol.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=57)** As you can see here, this basic idea of inheritance is really just a minor syntax change between languages.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=64)** Now when it comes to the details of overriding, that is allowing the subclass to replace the implementation of a method from the superclass.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=73)** The details are a bit too specific to each language to be useful here.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=77)** Some languages require keywords, others don't.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=80)** Some languages require keywords in both the superclass and subclass and so on.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=85)** For details on overriding, you'll need to refer to your chosen language.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=90)** That said, when common need is for code within the subclass, the new child class, to call a method that was originally defined in the superclass, its parent, and the word you'll see for that is super.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=104)** From within one of the methods of our CargoShuttle class, if I want to execute the setShield method that's defined in its superclass Spaceship, using Java I would call super dot and then the method name, so super.setShield.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=121)** Dot net languages like c# tend to use the word base instead of super because they use the terms base class and derived class rather than superclass and subclass.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=132)** But the idea behind it is the same.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=134)** Swift uses the word super, as does Python and Ruby.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=139)** That's the most common way of doing it.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=141)** However, c++ is a bit different because c++ allows for multiple inheritance, which means I could be inheriting from multiple classes.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=151)** So I can't simply say super and know that it will automatically go to the right superclass.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/using-inheritance?u=76281980&t=158)** In c++ I have to use the official name of whatever superclass I am referring to.

#### Abstract and concrete classes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've defined several different types of spaceships for our game. The starfighter, the cargo shuttle
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=6)** - and the warp cruiser. Weeeee.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=10)** - And all three of those inherit from the same common superclass called spaceship.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=15)** We'll use those three subclasses to instantiate objects for each specific type of ship but we'll never actually need to instantiate the generic concept of a spaceship.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=25)** In this case, spaceship can be defined as an abstract class because it exists purely for the sake of being inherited by other classes to provide some shared behavior but an abstract class itself can never be instantiated because it contains at least one abstract method which is a method that's declared with a method signature but not actually implemented in the abstract class.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=51)** That's deferred to any subclasses that inherit from the abstract class. They're responsible for actually implementing the abstract methods.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=60)** In uml diagrams we identify spaceship as an abstract class by using italicized font.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=67)** Now, not every method in an abstract class has to be abstract. The abstract class can have a mix of abstract methods and methods that it actually implements.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=77)** If all three of our ship types have shields that work the exact same way, rather than making them each implement the same set shield method, we'll implement it once in the spaceship class and they'll all inherit it.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=90)** But, if each type of ship moves in a different way then each subclass will need to implement its own unique move method. So, we'll declare an abstract move method in our spaceship class. Again, italics are used to indicate that it's abstract and that abstract method serves as a sort of placeholder for each of the subclasses to let them know that they'll need to individually implement it And of course, each of the subclasses are free to implement any additional methods that are unique to them.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=121)** Some languages like Java or C sharp let you explicitly mark a class as abstract when declaring it which prevents the language from allowing that class to be instantiated.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=131)** You must inherit from it with another subclass, implement the abstract methods in that subclass and then you can use that subclass to actually instantiate an object.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=142)** In Java, you can also mark classes as final which has the opposite effect of abstract. A final class is meant to be instantiated and cannot be extended or inherited from.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=153)** This is often referred to as a concrete class because it implements any missing functionality from the abstract class. Now, not all languages have official keywords to identify abstract and concrete classes and you can treat a class as if it were abstract simply by omission, just don't create an object from it. In fact, we could write our Java code without ever formally marking classes as abstract and it would work just fine.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/abstract-and-concrete-classes?u=76281980&t=179)** The benefit of including keywords like abstract and final is to communicate your intentions for a class to other programmers. It let's them know whether or not a class was designed with inheritance in mind.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** abstract (22), class. (4), let (3), implements (2), case, (1)
> **Speakers:** - and (2), - we (1)
> **CLI Commands:** cargo (1)
> **Definitions:** defined as (1)

#### Interfaces
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=0)** - Interfaces are another common form of abstraction that's supported by many object-oriented languages.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=6)** An interface is a programming structure that declares a set of methods for a class to implement, but the interface itself doesn't contain any functionality.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=16)** There's no implemented code or behavior.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=18)** It's just a collection of method signatures to specify a service.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=23)** For example, in Java an interface would be written like this, using the word interface instead of class.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=30)** This interface is named Moveable and it declares one method called move.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=35)** But that method doesn't have a body.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=37)** Again, you're not allowed to put any functionality inside an interface.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=42)** When we defined a new class and choose to implement a specific interface, it's like signing a contract, promising that the new class we're defining will implement all of the methods in that interface.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=53)** But, we're free to implement the inner workings of those methods however we want as long as the method names, inputs, and outputs match the interface.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=63)** For example, if we define an Asteroid class that implements the Moveable interface, then Asteroid is required to implement the actual code for the move method.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=72)** - You know, it sounds to me like interfaces are just an extreme version of abstract classes where none of the methods are implemented and everything's abstract.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=82)** - Interfaces and abstract classes can seem similar at first.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=86)** But they serve different purposes.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=88)** Interfaces are used to represent a capability that a class implements.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=93)** Whereas and abstract class represents a type that another class can inherit from.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=98)** Consider the abstract Spaceship class that our CargoShuttle class inherits from.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=102)** It represents a type of object with an is a relationship for inheritance.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=108)** The CargoShuttle is a Spaceship.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=111)** Now, let's look at the Asteroid class next to it.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=114)** Side-by-side it's obvious that Spaceships and Asteroids both have a move method.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=119)** - [Man] But Asteroids and Spaceships are completely different things.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=122)** The only thing they have in common is that they both move around in space.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=126)** - [Woman] Right, it would not make sense for Asteroid to inherit from Spaceship.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=129)** An Asteroid isn't a type of Spaceship.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=132)** However, moving is a capability they both share.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=136)** So they could both implement a common interface for Movable objects.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=140)** In UML, we represent an interface using a box that looks similar to a class.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=145)** But we include a tag with double angle quotes to indicate that it's an interface.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=150)** And, for classes that implement this interface, we use the same arrow as inheritance, but with a dashed line instead of a solid line to indicate that it's implementing an interface rather than inheriting from a class.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=163)** Now while most languages only allow a class to inherit from one other class, interfaces don't have that restriction.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=170)** A class can implement multiple interfaces.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=173)** In our video game, the spaceships and asteroids will both need to be drawn on the screen at some point.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=178)** In addition to the Movable interface, we can create a second interface that they both implement called Drawable.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=184)** Now, we don't need to know how the Spaceship and Asteroid's draw method works under the hood.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=189)** It's just a black box.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=191)** We just know that for any object, even types of objects that we've never encountered before, as long as it implements the Drawable interface, it will have a draw method that we can call.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=201)** An example of when this would be useful is if we have a list or collection storing all of the objects in our game.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=208)** To update the display, we can iterate through each of the potentially unknown objects in that list and simply check if it supports the Drawable interface.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=218)** So we'll call its draw method to update it on the screen.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=221)** - That sure is handy.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=222)** - That's why many developers favor using interfaces to provide a formal list of methods to support.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=228)** In fact, there's a well known saying that good developers program to an interface not to an implementation because it's a developer's choice how to implement those methods rather than being provided with that code.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/interfaces?u=76281980&t=242)** If your chosen language supports interfaces, then I highly recommend becoming familiar with how they're implemented in that language because interfaces are often a more future friendly way of programming than using inheritance.

#### Aggregation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=0)** - Inheritance is just one type of relationship that can exist between objects.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=5)** The concept of aggregation is another type of object relationship in which one object is built of other objects.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=12)** - Like this fleet of spaceships.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=13)** We can think of a fleet as an object that contains a bunch of individual spaceship objects.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=19)** - [Male Instructor] Aggregation is often referred to as a has a relationship, as opposed to the is a relationship of inheritance.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=26)** I wouldn't say that the fleet is a spaceship, but I could say that the fleet has a spaceship.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=32)** - Only one ship?
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=33)** Well, that's a puny little fleet.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=35)** - Well, in this context, has a can implicitly suggest has many.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=40)** So a fleet has many spaceships.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=43)** - That's more like it.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=44)** - What we're exploring here is that a has a relationship is different from the is a relationship of inheritance.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=51)** In UML diagrams, we can represent the has a relationship for aggregation with an unfilled diamond.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=57)** So the diagram here represents a fleet object that contains a collection of spaceship objects.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=63)** Spaceship refers to an abstract class, so the fleet might contain a variety of different concrete spaceship classes.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=70)** As with other diagrams, we can optionally indicate multiplicity with an asterisk to say that one fleet can have anywhere from zero to many spaceships.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=80)** Now, although aggregation is commonly used, it may not always be worth showing on a diagram, because the object lifetimes of those spaceships are not tied to each other or the existence of the fleet.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=91)** - So what happens when we end the fleet?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=93)** Do the ships go away?
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=94)** - In the case of aggregation, no.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=97)** The spaceships each exist independently of the fleet, so destroying the fleet is like breaking up a band.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=103)** The individual ships can continue to exist and do their own thing.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=107)** So perhaps a better phrase for an aggregation relationship than has a is uses a, or uses many.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/aggregation?u=76281980&t=115)** The fleet uses many spaceships, and if the fleet ceases to fly together, the individual spaceships will continue to exist on their own.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - inheritance (1), - like (1), - [male (1), - only (1), - well (1)
> **Code Keywords:** continue (2), abstract (1), class, (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), refers to (1)
> **Env Vars:** uml (1)

#### Composition
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=0)** - A more specific form of aggregation is something called composition.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=5)** Like aggregation, composition is based around a has-a relationship between objects but it specifically implies ownership.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=13)** I might say that a spaceship has an engine, but more accurately, I should that the spaceship owns the engine.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=20)** Composition implies ownership and an engine has no meaning or purpose in the system without a spaceship.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=27)** - It's like attaching the object to one of these model ships.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=29)** The spaceship has a shield and, since it's a star fighter, it has a weapon.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=34)** Or I should say, it owns a weapon.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=37)** - Right.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=37)** The space ship is composed of several different objects that each have their own attributes and behaviors.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=43)** But a key difference between composition and aggregation is that in a composition situation, if the owning object is destroyed by, say, an asteroid ...
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=53)** - Or an Olivia.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=56)** - The contained objects are destroyed, too.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=59)** The ships engine, its shield, its weapons, those are all gone.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=63)** Now, although aggregation may not always be worth showing in a UML diagram, composition often can be and it's represented with a filled-in diamond.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=73)** If the lifetime of an object is dependent on another object's existence, that's usually worth showing, if for nothing more than to prompt the idea that, when you're defining the owning class, you may need to write constructor and destructor methods to take care of creating and deleting those internal objects.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=91)** As with aggregation we can use multiplicity symbols to indicate that a spaceship is composed of one or more engines.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/composition?u=76281980&t=98)** And we can show other objects that the spaceship has as well, giving it a shield and possibly some weapons, too.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Speakers:** - a (1), - it (1), - right (1), - or (1), - the (1)
> **Code Keywords:** class, (1)
> **Env Vars:** uml (1)
> **Analogies:** it's like (1)

#### Challenge: Jukebox class relationships
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-relationships?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=14)** For this challenge, you'll be identifying the relationships between classes.

> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=18)** If you have your own set of class diagrams from completing the previous challenges, then I encourage you to work from those.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=25)** Otherwise, feel free to use our class diagrams, shown here.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/programming-foundations-object-oriented-design-3/challenge-jukebox-class-relationships?u=76281980&t=30)** Your goal is to identify opportunities between the classes to implement things like inheritance, interfaces, aggregation, and or composition.
>
